Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly with your hand go to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger on it: and press it tightly until the blood is blocked under your finger, and nothing can escape from it.
Then place small olive cauteries and a larger number of them on the fire, and blow on them until they are thoroughly ignited.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is ruptured, and place the cautery on the vein after you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If the blood flows out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery and extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not stop doing this with one after another until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and do not cause another harm to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is drawn out, its ends contract, and the blood is severed. Or it should be tied with a strong thread. Or let medicines with the property of severing the blood be applied to it, and constrict it with a suitable pressure bandage.
He who administers its severance with a ligature, and constriction with cloth, or application of burning substances, and similar methods, does not fully heal it, except rarely.
If it happens to someone and there is no physician present, nor medicine: then begin by placing the index finger on the orifice of the vein itself, as we have mentioned, and constricting it very tightly, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound, and over the artery. And the finger should not be removed, always cooling it with very cold water, until the blood freezes, thickens, and is severed.
And during this time, one should consider what it needs from a cautery or medicine.
¶ On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds, and Similar Cases.
We have already spoken in the first chapter about every ailment in which it is appropriate to use fire for cauterization and burning medicines, its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauters.
And I have placed there chapters from the top of the head down to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner: to alleviate and provide the sought-after for the seeker.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, O sons, what is in this chapter concerning the fear that is mentioned in the first chapter about cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary to have a more severe caution, for in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood often occurs, through which life depends on the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or the opening of an ulcer, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and things similar to these: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and in most of them death occurs.
And I warn you not to let hesitation overcome you, for sometimes methods of people with methods of illnesses fall upon you in this art. For there are those among them who become angry with their sickness, and death is轻于他对自己的病症及其恶意的长期强烈的感受。 And when the illness is from fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his music, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is deadly.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your desire and your greed. And do not presume anything from it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which a praiseworthy succession leads.
And use it in the treatment of all your ailments, by prognosis and indication of that to which health appropriately leads.
For in that is your assistance for gaining fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you with His understanding, O children, and not deprive you of guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by dividing it into sections, according to what preceded in the chapter of Cauterization, from the neck down to the feet: so that your search for what you want from it and what you need may be alleviated.
Regarding the treatment of water that accumulates in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this illness often occurs in children at birth: or when the midwife grasps the child's head without gentleness.
And it also happens at times for a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this disease in anyone other than children: and death hastened to all of them that I saw: therefore it seemed to me to abandon the treatment of it.
And indeed I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head swelled every day until he could not sit up straight because of the size of his head, and the swelling increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed gathers either between the skin and the bone, or gathers beneath the bone on the surface.
And the operation for that, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you must make one slit in the middle of the head in width: and let the length of the slit be almost two finger breadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of the spatula.
If the moisture has increased more and the abscess is larger, then make two slits according to this form.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides: and the water is depressed when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and this is not hidden from you: then you must make three slits in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the wounds with cloths and poultices. Then fumigate it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents, and ointments.
And do not allow binding the head equally. And feed the patient with all dry food of little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to observe where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of fluid appear.
For sometimes it is more on the occiput, or on the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the accumulation of fluid appear to you: and cut it open as much as you can.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occurs, and the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Regarding the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs occur to the eyes or to the chest of someone, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment with medicines does not help: then the final treatment for that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, the patient should shave his head. Then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the arteries appear. Then the patient should compress his neck with any cloth. Then observe where the vein pulses. And the locations of both are the depressed areas behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some individuals.
Then mark with encaustic on both: and cut each with a scalpel on both sides sharply, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood exits with a pulsating flow, which leaps into the interior frequently.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a length of three fingers. Then mark with encaustic, and cut until you reach the bone.
And what is necessary to release from the blood is six ounces, and you may release more or less from it. All of this according to the amount that appears to you from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
Then attend to the wound. Then if anything remains at the bone from the cut, then cut it off, so that a warm abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with bandages of linen cloth.
Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
On the removal of arteries that are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When an old hemicrania or acute catarrhs occur in a person due to sharp humidities and heat in the temporal arteries, or severe and similar old sodas: and it is treated with methods of medical cure, but does not improve: then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation in their extraction is that the patient should shave the hairs that are in the temples and focus on the visible artery in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is rarely hidden except in a few people or due to extreme cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the patient bind his head with any of his cloths; then rub the area with a coarse cloth. Or steam the area with hot water until it appears to you clearly. Then take the spatula that I shaped slightly before. Then scrape the skin with it easily until you reach the artery. Then throw the hook into it, and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin, and make it emerge from the tissues beneath it on all sides.
If however the artery is thin: then wrap it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it the portion according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened, and drawn together, so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not fully stop.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, down to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a double, strong thread, of silk; or from the gut of a hare, so that rot does not set in too quickly before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the excess that is between the two ligaments. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife cautery up to the mouth: until its ends are cut, and it will remain in place for the same procedure.
And it is better to do this, unless the patient has a fever, or is of a heated temperament. For cautery is one of those things that helps to stop moist discharges. Therefore, it is better treatment.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old lint: and properly made poultices should be placed on top.
And after the dissolution, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and with lint, until it heals.
If during the operation a flow of blood occurs from an artery, then quickly proceed to sever it with a cautery, or fill the place with zegi: and hold your hand until the blood is severed. If none of those are available to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood凝固。请注意，"congeletur sanguis"的直接翻译是“the blood freezes”，但在医学背景下，更准确的表达是“the blood clots”。但由于指示严格要求不做任何解释或修改，我提供了字面翻译。不过，在实际使用中应理解为血凝固的意思。由于指令限制，这里不进行调整。 And embrocate the place with water of intense coldness until the sharpness subsides and is constricted as appropriate.
And because it is lighter and easier to extract the artery, you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to almadgem. However, they are much less sharp than the two knives. For if they are sharp like the two knives, cold quickly sets in for both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, because there is a little thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this method, the work is better, lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with cauterization: and place both very hot until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends may be lengthened. For they do not consolidate completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment of acute and persistent tearing from the eyes.
When the tears are constant and their flow comes from the veins that are above the skull externally: and no remedy is effective with medicines for this condition, and you see the face of the patient already reddened, and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead, like the crawling of ants: and their eyes are weakened, moist, so that their eyelashes are already corroded by corrosion: and their eyelids are heated by the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients required this procedure for their treatment, and named it cure with a Sword.
And it is that you instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on their forehead. Then make three equidistant cuts on the forehead along its length: and let the length of each cut be about two fingers, with one cut equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second along the length slightly from the movement of the eyebrows, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are on both sides: and keep your hand away from the junction of the two jaws, and let the total space of each incision be the width of three joined fingers: and have a piece of prepared sponge or many pieces of dried cloth with which to dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends, whose following form is this. From the incision which is on the temple side, to the second incision: and with it remove all the skin which is between the two incisions, with a scalpel, which is over the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp little knife, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with skin, and its blunt side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until it appears through the skin.
Then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood equal to that has flowed out, press the areas from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then place in every fissure lint from old cloth, and put on them small pieces soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And take care that a warm abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, remove the bandage, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment, and other things with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of sharp tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When sharp, pungent, persistent catarrhs occur in someone: and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their sight already impaired: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out of them, and there is a sharp, piercing pain deep in the head, followed by sneezing: then take note of these occurrences, that such conditions and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the better treatment for these is this. And it is that you instruct the patient to rub his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another shortly across its width, and begin with an incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision go all the way to the bone: and keep your hand away from the jaw muscles, which move during chewing: until when the bone is exposed: and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with an old spatula, or with threads from linen. Then bind it above with bandages: and soak the bandages with wine and oil, so that a warm abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it and see that the warm abscess is already decreasing: then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth: such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony: and prepare an ointment from them, and administer it for the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is of one of four types: either a mineral stone, or similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar substances; or it is some animal.
When a stone or stone-like substance that does not add to or grow in the ear falls into it, expose the ear to the sun: if you see the stone, then drip something from violet oil or sesamin into the ear. Then administer its extraction and make the person sneeze with condysi, and plug the nose upon the onset of sneezing, after which you place pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards.
For it often exits with this treatment.
If it does not exit, then administer its extraction with gesti, that is, fine pizigarolis: whose form is this.
If it exits with the gesti, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, slightly bent blind hook.
If therefore it does not exit through that, then make a tube from bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole very much, and pack what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then extract with your breath strong suction.
For it often exits along with what we have mentioned. If it does not exit this way, then take a small amount of helkalimbat or visko, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are captured, and place it on the end of the probe after you wrap a properly positioned cloth around it; then insert it into the ear hole easily after you dry the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not exit with all that we have mentioned, then hurry to incise before a warm abscess or spasms occur.
And the method of the incision is to let blood from the patient first in the cephalic vein: and draw out blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and find a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed area: and let the incision be of a crescent shape until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever is possible for you from the instruments.
Then clean the incision quickly with your own speed, and care for it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its removal.
And if it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, fine spatula according to this shape.
And administer with it the cutting of that kind from the grains which fall into the ear, and do not do this when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place it in many pieces: then remove them with hooks, or with a fine thread, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In the water that enters the ear, the patient must use sneezing with condysium at first: and he has already filled his ear with some old cooked substance: and he is lying on that ear in which there is water. If it exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, soft, and heat them with fire for a moment, and let the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and hop on his foot from that side, and strike with another stone on the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, he should not stop doing this with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out in such a way that you take one from a parpyro, or from a reed, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire until most of it is burned. Then use another reed, and do this multiple times until all the water comes out: and extract it with a tube as described earlier with the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is of a small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which appears to the sense: then administer its extraction with hooks and forceps: and its matter is easier than all those held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your cure does not succeed: then you must look into the ear in the sun. If therefore something of the worms appears there, then extract it with hooks or with subtle forceps.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the slender end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate it: then suck with a strong suction. Do this many times until all the worms come out.
If however they do not obey you to come out, then coat the tip of the cannula with wax, as I said with the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already used to kill worms: and you will find this in the treatment of distillations.
And let your pouring of oil and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, narrower below, with a small hole, wider above.
Let it be that the piston which is in the concavity of the tube is properly made of bronze. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap cotton at its end with a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you choose into the tube: and place it in the ear; then insert the rod with the cotton above, and press it with your hand with moderate compression until the oil is pushed into the auditory concavity, and the patient feels it internally; and what is infused into the ear should already be warmed slightly by fire: and beware that what is distilled into it is not very cold; for the medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
Regarding the care of auricular ailments caused by foreign objects.
Sometimes some infants come out of their mothers' wombs not perforated.
And it has also happened to some people that blockage in the ear canal occurred due to a wound or flesh grown in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs in the deep part of the ear canal, which is not visible: and it sometimes happens near the outer part of the canal, which is visible.
Therefore, that which occurs in the deep part of the ear and is not visible, is in many cases difficult to treat.
And in that which is visible, you should place the patient's ear in the light and look into it: then if you see a visible blockage, open it with a delicate spatula, whose form is as follows, let its end have some small width, part of which is sharp: and the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the growth is from flesh born in the ear canal: then hold it with a fine hook, and cut it with intense ease, until all the flesh is removed.
If the growth is in the depths of the ear: then take a fine, gentle probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel the growth with that which the patient experiences with heaviness of hearing, attempt to open it with ease: and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear lint according to the size of the opening which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take lint, and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle ground zedoary over it, and use it.
If you see the ear already forming an abscess: then it is necessary to remove the lint, and allow it with another piece of lint that you wrap in cerote made with rose oil, until the warm abscess settles: then care for it until it heals.
If however a flow of blood should occur: then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water, and place it on the affected ear: and use the remaining treatment which stops the flow of blood as described in the chapter on distillations.
Regarding the treatment of Altheli of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
The injuries that occur to the eyelids, sometimes they are moist, and they are dry.
Therefore, you must hold them with forceps: or with a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots. Then place ground zegi on the affected areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lens-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them after the incision is better. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Regarding the treatment of warts on the eyelids.
Chapter Nine.
Often something occurs on the eyelids that resembles warts in its strength and hardness, and because of this, it is called a granum: and it is an aggregation of coarse humidity in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
The operation on them is to examine whether there is a granum protruding from the eyelid, which can move in all directions, and its removal is easy.
Therefore, make an incision over it lengthwise. Then remove it from all sides with ease until it comes out. Then hang her with a hook, and cut her off.
If the excision is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight incision, then it harms the patient to do this in some cases.
If the fissure is large, then join it with stitching and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it and causes it to flesh over.
And if the stone is inclined towards the inner part of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and hang the stone with a hook; besides which you may need an incision and press it from all sides.
If the eyelid is extended by an incision, it does not harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the stone is cut out with salt water: and care for the area with what causes it to flesh over, until the patient heals.
Regarding the treatment of Axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is fat that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid: and it happens most frequently to children: and it burdens their eyes, causing them to have catarrhs in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears quickly come to them, and you see them always lying on their faces, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are wet, which are already covered by swelling: and the prominence of Axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of performing it is for the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare ointment on it, and make a circle out of that ointment according to the amount that Axirnath contains on all sides. Then place him on it, and press with your fingers on all sides so that Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel penetrating cracks across the width, and let not the crack be deeper than that which is made in bloodletting.
Or it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach Axirnath, for in many conditions Axirnath proceeds from a crack in the shape of a fragment of white fatty tissue.
Therefore, remove it with the cloth that you have inserted between your fingers to the outside: and you turn your hand to the right and left until it disperses: and beware, lest you add to the crack, so that you do not reach the eye with the scalpel and injure it.
If Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the crack, you should extend the crack slightly with ease until Axirnath proceeds. Then remove it as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are people who rub salt and place it in the concavity of the crack, so that what remains of that moisture may liquefy.
Then care for it until it heals.
If, however, a warm aposteme occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Regarding the methods for ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid softens, causing it to droop more than it should.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the natural course and become aged: then they harm the eye and cause various illnesses to occur, such as continuous tears, softening of the eyelids, and opacities, until it becomes a cause for the destruction of the eye.
And ataxmir of the eye is treated in four ways. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization. Or ataxmir is treated by incision and suturing. Or by canes, as I will describe.
It is necessary to place the head of the patient in your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it turns, that is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, at the part where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and place it near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a probe.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with the spatula applied from the larger lacrimal to the smaller lacrimal.
Then extract the thread, and place a small piece of felt or cloth under the eyelid; then mark above the eyelid with wax, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it should be done according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: for it already varies among people.
For there are those who need a large quantity to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that is softened from the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find between the two lines you marked, and begin from the larger lacrimal to the smaller lacrimal, and let the cut be near the natural hairs, as thick as a rod.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape the entire area. Then sew together the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread: and clean the blood, and make adhere what remains of the threads over the two eyebrows, with some adhesive substances, if you wish to do so: and if not, do not bother.
Then leave the suture and threads for three days, or four: afterward care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and care for it with something that dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its gathering together: but it will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is careful attention to the patient, and it is from good practice, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir as well: and it is to mark on the eyelid a figure that resembles the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have mentioned.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or connected hooks, according to this following shape, then cut away the excess with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal incisions.
If it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not align: then take a needle with thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and gather the threads between your fingers equally.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation: and raise the skin on which the comma is marked, as we have mentioned.
Then bring together the two edges of the wound with suture: and care for it until it heals.
If an abscess occurs at your incision or tear: treat the abscess with cerote, and similar substances, from soothing ointments.
And it sometimes happens to the eyelid that its hairs turn inward.
Therefore, you should use what we have said about incision in it; but缝合两者更需要技巧。 On the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds.
And there is a case where you turn the eyelid and split the fissure which is inside, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two reeds or two fine sticks: whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you now incise at the ends of each on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully place each one over the excess of the eye's eyelid, and tighten two reeds on both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For skin that is tightly bound will die, and turn black, and rot, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When it therefore consolidates, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not pierce.
And this is the form of the two reeds that you will make according to this figure, and the size in length and width.
Chapter XII.
On the removal of piercing hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation is to take a fine needle and insert into it a thread made of fine, soft silk. Then join the ends of it, and tie each end with a very fine knot; and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then arrange another fine thread under it briefly, and knot the ends of it if you wish.
Then let the patient put his head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation is clear; for it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up over the thread: and insert into the thread that hair: whether it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the thread from the eyelid with the natural hair.
If the thread is pulled and the hair does not come out with it: pull the thread downward with the thread you arranged in it until the thread comes out from the upper opening, and see it with your eyes: then pull the thread and the hair: extract both, and close the eye: leave it closed for two days or three until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either attach to it a longer hair from the natural hairs and lift with it: or leave it until it grows longer over time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore occurs from the healing of a wound, or a cut, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of the operation in this is to cut open that healing, and to separate its edges, and place a strip of linen between them, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things that dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use in it things that soften, such as Greek hay: and foment with water, in which marshmallow and flax seed have been cooked: and an unguent of dyaquilon, which has been melted with some oil: and wrap with it a strip of linen, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is to strive with all ingenuity that it does not heal in the same form as it was at first.
And this operation which we have described is only a correction of the Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return entirely as it was.
The treatment of the Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and it is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is what is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or a cut, or a cautery, and by things similar to these.
And the way of the operation in it is to take a needle, on which there is double thread, and dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left lacrimal to the right lacrimal, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If the figure of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take the awl, and place it in the cut, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two cuts be from the two angles of the incision, until they converge and form an angle: until when they come together they form this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that its sharp side is below, from what follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with wool thread.
Then care for them with what we have said about soothing medicines and plasters until they heal.
And if an Alxatrati occurs from a cut, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary to make a flat incision under the eyelash hairs, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with an ointment, as we have said.
And the sum of the discourse on the treatment of Alxatrati, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed accordingly, as it is prepared according to the form of Alxatrati.
For it is indeed of many diversities in form: and the prudent craftsman prepares his method with whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or near it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in most dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Regarding the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, you must introduce the end of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adherence with a blunt spatula that is not sharp like a regular spatula, but slightly dull: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula does not injure the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be such that you pare away that adherence easily, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adherence is healed.
Then pour salty water into the eye, wash it with water: or drip onto it from a sieve made from dissolved zinc and water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with flax oil: and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye: and after the third day use a sieve, and with plasters, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of the claw and the prominence of the flesh of the tears.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the claw is according to two methods. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to a hard, thin sieve. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when touched by hot iron, or when you try to take it with a hook, breaks apart, and the hook does not hold onto it.
And both kinds do not begin unless from the larger tear duct, extending gradually until it covers the sight and prevents light and eye movement.
And the method of operating on its incision is: that the patient places their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the claw with a hook of few turns, and extend it upward. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from horsehair, or oxhair, or cowhair: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle temporarily, and fix it in the middle of the claw: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the claw with the thread, and extend it upward: and scrape with the hair the side of the claw that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with the hair to its end.
Then cut the remainder in the root of the lacrimal with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural lacrimal flesh, so that its habitual course of tears is not impeded.
And sometimes the claw is extended with a hook alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then it should be scraped with a gentle spatula, whose form is this.
And beware that the spatula does not touch the horny membrane, and that no rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, put some ground salt into the eye, or something made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then care for it with what is necessary until it heals.
If the claw is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a hook be fixed in it, then this kind of claw is often cured with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye, and take a fine, gentle spatula of weak sharpness according to this form. And scrape the claw gently from above with finesse. If you see that it becomes sparse and breaks, and the scraper impresses on it, then drip into the eye from your hour something made from ziniar, or red powder, or ground salt: and cover the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure on the same operation until all of it is removed. Unless there is a warm apostema contrary to your eye: then let it be, and treat the apostema until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it reasonably until it heals.
If the prominence of the lacrimal flesh harms the eye greatly, it should be suspended with a hook, and part of it should be cut off: and do not make the incision too deep, so as not to affect the course of the tears.
Then apply a red stillicide to the lacrimal, or one made from zinnia, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in the eyes of some people, red flesh is formed or positioned over them until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a pomegranate flower.
And the procedure for it is that the patient should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps or tweezers.
Then cut the flesh initially with a scalpel until the entire thing is finished. And be careful of the eye so as not to harm it during the operation: and let your cut, whether you make it with a spatula, which we mentioned in the hoof incision, or with small scissors, with which asbel is gathered, according to how its form will come after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and all the almond is removed, then fill the eye with ground salt: or sprinkle on it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye outward linen with egg white: so that it is secured from a warm abscess.
If any of the almond remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a warm abscess: then leave the eye alone, and treat it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it is healed.
And similarly treat the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary to keep the treatment of any weak eye away from the natural course or the accidental course by operating with iron: and do not involve your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
Regarding the removal of snow from the eye.
Chapter Eighteen.
Snow are the red veins that form over the eye and prevent it from functioning properly: and they weaken the eye over time.
Therefore, you must first consider whether the eye in which there is snow is strong and has no other illness besides the snow: then remove the snow from it.
And you should instruct the patient to place their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be subtle of doubling, according to this form, or let there be two hooks set step by step in one body, according to this form.
Then gather with fine scissors those veins with subtlety, and cleanse the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they dissolve: and beware lest you injure the eye with the ends of the spatula.
And let your operation be at noon facing the sun, and be firm in your operation very much: so that you cut only those veins.
And when you have completed, then distill into the eye sieg green or red: so that with its sharpness it corrodes what remains of sebel.
If however it is not possible for you to gather it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with something that relieves the pain of the eye: and leave it for several days, until its pain is relieved: and secure it from a warm abscess: then again proceed upon it according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors.
Regarding the reduction of Arissati to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians name Arissati as a fistula, which you treat with a cautery or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what the preceding account has stated.
And if it is not healed, then there is no efficacy in it: unless you incise above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the opening is exposed.
When therefore the tooth is uncovered, and you see in it corruption or blackening, then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is called Head of Asper.
Let it be made of Indian iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which should be pointed with the sharpening of a file, or with the subtle pointing of an aliskilfer: and place it on the corrupted part of the bone.
Then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand for a moment until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying and astringent medicines.
If therefore the area is stabilized, and flesh grows there, and the course of pus is stopped: and it remains for forty days, and does not recur, and does not form an abscess, and no accident occurs in it: then you know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no ingenuity unless to redirect the fistula to the nasal opening, according to this form.
And it is such that you uncover the second bone with iron or with a sharp medicine: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill according to this form, let the iron end be triangular: and its wood should be a tapered pyramid down to the end, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and move your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the awl until it penetrates the bone, and let the patient feel it; by this he finds air escaping from it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Then restore the area with styptic and drying medicines, as I have taught you.
When the flesh is formed and the area hardens, then the material which was flowing outward returns to the nose; therefore, this is a milder condition for the patient.
Regarding the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter Twenty.
When the eye protrudes entirely and no harm or reduction occurs in vision: then you must loosen the patient; thereafter, bleed him; afterwards, apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply to the eye an unguent made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye over the plaster with many bandages.
And the method of the bandages is as follows: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each cloth over four times; make many of these; then place them one upon another according to the amount needed for the eye; then bind them with a strong tie using a hard cloth.
And repeat cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping and leave the eye tightly bound day and night.
If however the ligature softens again, then it must be tightened greatly; then remove the ligature after a day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the grape skin, it is good: and if not, then apply the plaster, and the powders, and the compression, and the suction cups, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of the grape skin.
Chapter Twenty-One.
When a disruption occurs in the grape skin layer, then it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grain of a grape, and the appearance of the person is marred because of it, and you wish to cut it: then the procedure for it is as I narrate: and it is to insert a needle at the root of the grape-like protrusion from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle with double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle in its place.
Then cut the double thread, and tie it around the grape-like part with the needle: and tighten it greatly.
Then remove the needle, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye: and leave it until the thread falls off with the grape-like part.
Then care for the eye with what strengthens it until the wound heals.
And sometimes the grape-like protrusion is tightened on all sides with a circle made of cloth until the eye develops a large prominence.
Then it should be pierced with a fine spatula that reaches the depth of the eye, and let the white moisture run, and the eye descend, and immediately proceed: then bind it until it heals.
On the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
This ailment which is called Alcomnati is not anything but a pus that gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is to make the patient sit on a prepared seat or stand straight.
Then take his head in your hands from two sides and move it until you see the pus come to the lower parts of your eyes. Then it should be fixed, and not allowed to descend, and the light should be completed: and the patient should see things as he saw before: and if it does not descend further, you know that it is water.
If the pus does not prepare to descend with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula and use it to find the area above the corneal membrane near the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal layer in the crown, until the pus comes out.
When it comes out, then drip warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled Greek hay and honey: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of water descending into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already spoken about the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing, declaring and explaining it, then receive the same according to health.
Then therefore you must have the patient sit between your hands, facing the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye tightly, and hold it very tight: then with your right hand if the eye with water is the left eye, or with your left hand if the eye is the right, hold his eyelid.
Then take with your right hand the needle if the eye is left: or with your left hand if the eye is right.
Then place the tip of the needle near the cornea at the thickness of the radius where the white of the eye is clearly visible next to the smaller lacrimal: then push the needle with force: and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the sclera of the eye: and you will feel that the needle has reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measurement of the needle's journey to the depth be the distance from the pupil to the end of the black part: and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see it in the pupil of the eye itself, due to the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then place the needle until it reaches the location where the water is. Then press it below with one hand after another.
If water descends immediately, then the patient sees it above where he opens his vision immediately, and the pupil in his eye.
Then rest for a little while. If water descends, then place it second, except where the pupil emerges.
When it is fixed, and does not rise: then extract the pupil with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something from clear gem salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place the entire cotton or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white outside the eye: and bandage the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, put ground cumin mixed with egg white on it.
If the pupil does not obey you for entering into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a person whose eye is very hard: then you must take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose form is this.
Therefore, insert it only into the joint with it, and do not push your hand into the hole: and you do this only to make a path to the delicate place of the pupil.
Then insert the pupil as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, a properly made bedroom should be prepared for the patient, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room: and he should be prohibited from all movements, and from coughing: and give him food that soothes his nature: and he should not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement up to the third day.
Then dissolve it in that dark house, and test its vision: and make it see things. Then reduce the bandage up to the seventh day.
And it should not be done at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather, it should be delayed, because water rises quickly through a vehement sight.
If a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to treat the eye before the seventh day: and regulate it with what calms the abscess until it is calmed: then restore its sight, and place over its face a cover to regulate its sight under it for several days, while being in that dark house.
Then let him gradually go out of the house and move about in his own rooms.
And know that in zaltea the disciple is not exempt from seeing several times: then he may dare to perform the operation.
And indeed it has come to our notice from some who are from Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a perforated vessel was made through which water is drawn.
Therefore, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of figures from the vessel, so that one who is ignorant may stand over them.
They shall be made of proper metal: and their ends shall be shaped with the same fineness into triangles, and the end sharp.
Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes diverse kinds of flesh grow in the nose, among which there is something that resembles a many-footed scorpion: and from it is a cancerous, stony, brownish colored flesh: and there is also one which is soft flesh, not brownish in color.
In those among these kinds of flesh that are soft, not deceitful, nor cancerous, you must have the patient sit between your hands facing away from the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into those flesh growths: then pull them outwards, and cut with a sharp fine spatula from one side what you have grasped of them until you know that all the flesh is now removed.
If any part remains that cannot be cut off: then scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. If blood dominates you, or a warm abscess occurs: then oppose it with what is appropriate; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then quickly cauterize it until the blood is severed and all the flesh is removed.
Then after the incision, vinegar, and water, or wine should be thrown into the nose. If the nose is opened and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then you know that he is now healthy.
If moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that flesh has grown within the upper nasal bones to which the instrument does not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to introduce a linen thread which has some thickness, and many knots on it: place the length of a finger, or less, between each knot: the patient should be instructed to insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe, or whatever is possible for him after he makes it similar to azer: and he should pull it, drawing its smell until it reaches the throat and comes out above his throat.
And indeed, boys in schools often do something similar to this: and it is easy for one who wants to.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then with the knots on the thread, carefully cut the flesh. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then withdraw the thread: and place in the nose after wiping the blood a lint that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead tube at the end of the nose for several days until it heals.
If however it needs a treatment which dries, use that one.
For making a head purgation device, through which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: let it be like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its spout similarly.
And if you want to make a closed tube, like a cane.
And the shell of the instrument for making head purgation should be full, open: to which there is a handle at the end of it, with which you hold it when you heat oil or any juice and liquid substances in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
Often a polyp grows at the end of the nose, and it enlarges and increases with days until its appearance becomes ugly.
And because of this, it is necessary to cut it down at the beginning of its appearance and root out all of it. Then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that serves in its place.
If the cutting is delayed until it enlarges, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, and has little sensation: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
For I have often seen that cutting into such an abscess caused great harm to the person who had it.
And if the aposteme is of mild touch, not of dark color, and you see that a cut is possible in its entirety, then evacuate the diseased area, and cut it without fear, without dread: and treat the place with that which dries and binds until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On sewing the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when there is a rupture of continuity in one of these cartilages, the operation is of little benefit, unless in certain individuals.
Therefore, when something like that happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood: then bring together the edges of the wound with stitching: then treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the ruptured continuity are already separated and each part has become healed: then it is necessary to scrape off all the part from its visible skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two edges with stitching and bind them tightly, and sprinkle dragon's blood powder and crushed frankincense over it: then place a piece of diaphoretic plaster over the powders, and another consolidating plaster: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of stitching is to bring together the rupture of continuity either with needles, as we have mentioned in sewing the abdomen: or bring together the rupture with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of lip knots.
Many men suffer from hard swellings similar to grains of a herb usually on the inner parts of their lips: and some of them are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lip inside out, and cut above every knot, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from all sides.
Then fill the place after the incision with ground zegi until the blood is stopped.
Then it should be treated with vinegar, and the area where there is astringency should be cured with it until the wounds heal.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of the flesh growth above the gum.
Often flesh grows above the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore, you must suspend it with a hook: or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus flow, or the blood.
Then place ground zegi or other astringent, drying powders on the area.
If that flesh returns after the treatment, for it often returns, then cut it a second time and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
On the filing of teeth with iron.
Sometimes on the surfaces of the teeth, both from the inner and outer part, and between the gums, rough, ugly crusts form, and they sometimes blacken and turn yellow and green until corruption from that reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul because of it.
Therefore, you must make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the mill tooth and the tooth where the crusts appear, until nothing of them remains, and similarly do for the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and others until they are cleaned.
If therefore what is in them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until ultimately your effort achieves what you wish.
And you should know that a tooth requires scrapers of various forms and many shapes: according to how they are prepared for your operation: therefore, the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to different forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared and available to you, as you see.
Chapter XXX.
Chapter on the Extraction of Tooth Pain.
You must treat the tooth for its pain with all ingenuity and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing to replace it when it is extracted, since it is of a noble substance; until there is no excuse for its extraction, then you must extract it when the patient is compelled, so that the painful tooth may be secured and confirmed by you.
For often the pain deceives the sick person, and they think that it is in a sound tooth and extract it; then the pain does not abate until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have now seen that from the operation of the bloodletters.
When therefore it is confirmed with you that the painful tooth itself is the cause, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula, which has some efficacy, until the gum loosens from all sides; then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps firmly on it, and position the head of the patient between your knees; hold it well so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight out so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it under it on all sides with ease, and attempt to move it as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded: then you must fill that hole with a cloth: and grasp it with the tip of a slender probe, so that it does not break when you constrict it with forceps.
And you must carefully perform the scarification around the gum on all sides extensively.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it, leaving a part behind, which may return to the patient with an even worse maliciousness than the original pain.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness in extracting it, besides administering what we have mentioned.
For many times they cause great illnesses to occur in people, whose roots are very difficult to break and remain all of them or some of them: or to extract it along with some bones of the jaw; as we have testified many times.
Then it should be treated after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then grind something from spigelia, and fill the area with it: and if not, cauterize it if spigelia is not available to you.
The forms of delicate forceps with which the tooth is moved should have long ends, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not double when pressed on the tooth: the handles should be thick as you see, so that when you grip with them, they do not bend or double: the ends should be short and made of Indian iron or properly made steel, with straight ends: and at their ends there should be teeth, some of which enter others and grip firmly with a proper constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made in the shape of the mouth of a bird called a stork, and they have a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When something remains after the removal of a tooth that is broken, then it is necessary to place fat with butter on the area for one day or two days until the area softens: then insert algasti or forceps into it, whose ends resemble the mouth of a bird called a stork. This is the form of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file inside: or like an aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not obey you to come out with these forceps, then you must make a cavity above the root, and expose all the flesh, and introduce an instrument which is called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let the end be short and somewhat thick: and let it not be brittle, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root comes out through that, it is good: and if not, assistance must be given with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first triangular end, in which there is some thickness.
The form of the delicate triangular end.
And with other instruments and tools which have been mentioned previously in the reasoning of teeth.
It is also assisted with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be brittle.
And know that the instruments of teeth are many: and similarly other instruments are not counted, and a wise craftsman, subtle in his art, finds the instrument by himself according to what the operation and the diseases themselves signify.
For there are sharp instruments to which the ancients did not speak, due to the diversity of their kinds.
If the bone of the jaw is fractured or any other bones of the mouth are broken or become putrefied: then examine it in its place with what is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned for the extraction of roots, and assist with levers, whose form is this.
Let it have something thick gradually so that it may be grasped or held firmly with the bone, and does not slip away, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable for it.
If there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction and care for it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII.
On filing teeth that protrude over others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the appearance is marred by this: and especially when it happens to women and girls, that is, those who are for sale.
Therefore, you must consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or its filing or smoothing is not possible: then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak and is made of hardened iron with a very sharp end.
And let your incision be made over it for many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
If it is protruding and needs filing: then file it with an Indian file, whose shape is this.
Let the entire file be from India, and its handle of strong metal points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is gradually filed over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out: then smooth it, at the end scrape it with some razors.
If there is a tooth from which a part is broken and it hurts the tongue: then it must be filed until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is evened out: and flattened, so that it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
About the rotation of teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth are shaken or jarred by a blow or fall: and the patient cannot hold what he eats so that it does not fall out: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the treatment was ineffective: then the method is to bind them with gold wire: because silver turns green and rots after days: but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of medium thickness according to the size that fits between the teeth.
And the form of the rotation is to take the wire and insert its doubled end between two healthy teeth: then weave with the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the thread all the way to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then again weave the thread up to the part from which you started: and hold your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not completely removed: and let your constriction as you pull the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not escape.
Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors: and bring together both, and turn them with a gesture: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so that they do not hurt the tongue: then leave it tightened as such while remaining.
If it loosens or is cut, tie them with another thread, and it will remain stable in this way for the entire time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of binding two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And it sometimes happens that one or two teeth return to their places after falling out and are retained as we have described and remain: and only a wise and experienced craftsman can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow's bone is adapted: and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in the position where the tooth is secured and tightened, as we have said: and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from an injury that has already healed.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient on his head in your hand, and lift his tongue: then cut that nervous ligament across until the tongue is loosened from its restraint.
And if there is something hard and knotted, and it is from the healing of the wound: then apply a hook to it, and cut it across until you sever the ligament and loosen the knot.
And beware that the cut is not deep in the flesh, and do not cut the artery there, lest a flow of blood occur.
Then it should be treated at the site of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a strip of linen that the patient should hold throughout the night: so that it does not harden again.
If a flow of blood occurs, then apply ground zeggium to the area. If the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a suitable cautery: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess forms under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function: and it may grow so large as to fill the mouth.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient facing the sun: and examine the abscess.
If therefore you see it of dark color or black, hard, to which the sick person does not feel: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it inclines to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then apply a hook to it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from all sides. If the blood overpowers you during your operation, then apply ground zeggium until the bleeding stops; then return to your operation until you remove it completely.
Then it should be treated with vinegar and salt: and care for it with the appropriate further treatment until it heals.
Regarding the treatment of abscesses of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat glands, which resemble algodad occurring externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it: or it will burst on its own.
And if it is of white color, round, and its root is thin: then it must be cut open.
And the operation on it is that you consider before his operation if the warm aposteme has fully subsided or decreased somewhat: then make the patient sit facing the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue below with an instrument, which has this form.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is fine like a little knife.
When therefore you press his tongue with it and the aposteme is shown to you and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outward as much as you can, besides which pull out some of the tissues with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, which has the following form. It resembles scissors: however, its ends are curved so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other's尖端：因此务必使其由刚铁或吸水的alfuled制成。（Note: There seems to be a translation error or anomaly towards the end of the sentence which mentions "sharp end" in Chinese, interrupting the flow of the English translation. Also, "indo" and "alfuled" are likely mistranslations or archaic terms not directly translatable.)
And if this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose form is this. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, the patient should gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate bark has been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar astringents, until the bleeding is stopped: then care for it until it heals.
And at times, other abscesses arise in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them accordingly as I have said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I have treated a woman with an abscess that had arisen in her throat, which was drawing her to darkness, loss of senses, nearly closing her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her from eating and drinking water: and she had nearly reached death, if it had lasted for one more day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, extending above the two nasal openings.
I hurried quickly and attached a hook to one of them. Then I pulled it out, and a large part was pulled from it.
Then I cut it off where it was grasped from the nostril opening.
Then I did that which protruded from the other nasal opening.
Then I opened his mouth and pressed his tongue: then I fixed a hook in the apostema: then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood came out: therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she began to drink water immediately, then she took some food.
Therefore, I did not cease to cut from that apostema many times over a long period: and the apostema succeeded in place of what I was cutting, until it was extended too much and with it.
I therefore devised and cauterized the apostema in the throat.
It therefore stood from the addition: then I walked away, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
Regarding the incision of the apostema of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula and it becomes swollen, and is long: then it is called perpendicular. And if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division and the treatment does not help: and you see that the warm apostema has subsided from it and is thin: then you must cut it.
And which is a rounded mass from it, and it has no length, and it is of bloody color, or brown, or black, and it has no sensation: then you must avoid cutting it. For in it there is fear over the sick.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described by its whiteness and length, make the sick person sit before the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument of which I previously spoke: then fix a hook in the uvula, and pull it down: and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for incising both tonsils.
And you must not cut from it except that which extends beyond its natural limit without addition.
For if you cut more from it, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have mentioned in the incision of both tonsils: and care for it until it heals.
If the sick person is terrified of the incision: then you must use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and pain.
And the method of cauterization for it is not without sharp water: and it is to place the sick person's head in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take from the water I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unextinguished ash, and place it not too thick nor too thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, which has this form.
Let the end on which the medicine is placed be concave, like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with medicine on the ulcer itself: and let the patient lie on his side, so that saliva may run out of his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you apply it to the ulcer for a quantity of half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the irritation of the medicine calmed.
And if you want to take a cloth, and wrap it around the end of the rod, and pour the cloth with medicine, and insert the rod with the cloth into the canal above, until the cloth adheres to the ulcer.
Do this many times until you achieve what you wish from the cauterization of the ulcer. Then leave it: for it dries up and falls off after three days, or four.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, clean around the ulcer with a cloth soaked in butter, and cleanse what remains of the medicine around it: and mix it with cold water.
And it should be treated externally with embrocations and internally with gargles until it heals.
Therefore, this method of treatment is more beneficial than incision and farther from fear.
And sometimes the uvula is also treated with something finer than cautery and incision: it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamus, hyssop, origanum, ruta, sseah, chamomile, abrotane, and similar herbs; and gather all of these or some of them in a pot, and let them be submerged in vinegar and boiled: and the pot should be sealed with suitable clay: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot on which a concave instrument is placed according to this method.
Let it be made of silver or bronze: and insert the end with the knob into the mouth of the sick person until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is greatly darkened.
