Then I examined all the openings: and I found them leading to each other from all parts of the leg.
I hurried and cut above one of those openings until I exposed part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: already corroded, blackened, rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated through to the marrow.
I removed what was exposed to me and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I ensured that there was no corruption left in the bone other than what I cut and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I placed the wound's restoration with consolidating medicines for a long period. And it was not consolidated.
Then I examined and exposed above the bone a second time beyond the first exposure: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I removed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
I exposed it again and did not cease to cut the bone bit by bit, and attempted its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut about one palm's width from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored him with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and this repetition was not necessary in his operation, and his cutting, unless because of the disposition of the patient's weakness, and the paucity of his tolerance, and my fear over him of death: for it happened to him at all hours from the superfluity of evacuation evil swoons.
He was therefore healed with a complete healing: and hard flesh was born in the place of the bone, and his condition was made good in his body, and his strength returned: and he moved about in his conditions: and walking did not harm him at all contrary to his state.
If however a prominent bone in the body is already fractured: then it is necessary to suture it, according to this method: and it is: to take a bandage, and bind it at the end of the prominent bone: and command someone who stretches it upwards: and place another bandage from wool thicker than the first bandage. Then bind it over the flesh that is on the bone: and extend its end so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you cover the flesh over the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not harm the flesh.
Place wood or a board under the lower bone appropriately: for when you do this, it does not prevent cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be on the corrupted place for a while, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone: and it does not appear to the sense in its appearance, and another is compelled to take its place in sawing.
If the bone is corrupted and not prominent, but rather its part is continuous with another part: and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all sides entirely. Then place the wood below.
Then cut it from the first side where there is corruption until the cutting continues from the other side: and let the cutting be according to the length from the corruption somewhat, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
If however the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except scraping.
If the corruption is in the carpal bones of the hand or in the tarsal bones of the foot: then the matter is exceedingly difficult. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it, and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever method is corrected for you: when the Vein or Nerve does not contradict you.
And know that the cutting instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their fineness, and their size, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare every kind of instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the kind of instrument you need when you have long study with you. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is testimony to the methods of diseases: then you will find by yourself that which is suitable for it from the instruments for every illness.
And I am confirming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will use as examples on which you will cut, and the test with which you will experiment on others.

Regarding the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities become putrefied, either from an external cause or an internal cause.
When you have treated that corruption with medical treatment and the cure does not help, and you see the corruption spreading to the limb, which nothing can reverse: then it is necessary to cut off that limb up to where the corruption follows, so that the patient may be saved from death or a worse malice than the loss of the limb.
And the sign that this has happened to someone is that the limb turns black until it rots: whether fire has burned it or it has rotted after turning black: until that rot spreads throughout the body, creeping into the entire limb.
Hurry therefore with its incision, and likewise if the cause of corruption is from the strike of some venomous creatures: such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and toad, and similar.
If therefore the corruption or strike is at the end of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption so that it may advance to the rest of the hand.
And likewise if it occurs in the hand, then cut it from the root of the wrist, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold of the arm's wrist.
And if it invades it, then cut the arm at the elbow joint.
If however the corruption spreads, and you see it around the elbow: then in that case the death of the patient is imminent. And to administer beyond this in the treatment is an excess of potency.
And likewise do for the foot when the corruption invades a toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold of the instep, then amputate the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
If the corruption has already advanced above the knee, then there is no remedy for it except amputation, and to leave the patient to death.
And the method of cutting off or sawing a limb is to tie a ligature below the part you wish to cut: and tie another ligature above the place. And the assistant extends one bandage to the lower parts: and the assistant extends another bandage upwards above. And you separate the flesh which is between the two bandages with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then it shall be cut or sutured.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and the blade should not touch a healthy area: and an additional injury and a hot swelling befall the patient.
If, however, internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the area quickly: or apply certain powders that stop the flow of blood. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, the same incident that I tell you about.
Which is that a blackening occurred on his foot, with a burning resembling the combustion of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began to affect the entire foot.
Therefore, the man quickly acted for himself after he saw the corruption occurring in his limb with its severity, which he discovered from the pain and the burning: and he cut it at the joint, and was healed.
And after a long time had passed, the same corruption occurred in his index finger.
He came to me therefore, and I tried to restore that excess with what I put on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body; and the excess did not return, and it began to move to another finger; until corruption began in the hand.
He called upon me to amputate his hand: and I refused to do so, hoping against the return of that excess: and I also feared for him death from the cutting of his hand. For the strength of the man was above the fall.
After he had despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and I received news of him that he hurried and amputated his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought this account except to be of help regarding that which falls under this kind of illness: and to serve as an indication by which it may be known, and according to which action may be taken.
Regarding the treatment of sinuses, and the nature of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy limbs, and the area of the abscess extends until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts or is perforated, and what is inside comes out as pus, and an empty space remains like a vessel: and the skin over it is like a cloth that has become thin: and it does not reach its ultimate corruption, nor impress upon bone, nerve, or ligament: it merits to be called a Sinus: and it is not named Fistula: however, if its area extends until the corruption affects one of these members, then it is named Fistula, or zuken.
And the care is that you cut all that skin as it rolls up: and especially when it has become thin, and made like cloth: and trust that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
And if you hope that the skin adheres, and that last corruption does not result from it: and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then care for it with aclyster: and it is that you should consider towards the sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus emitted from it has a foul odor: then purify it with Egyptian medicine, which is of vinegar, oil, and ziniar, equal parts received, gathered in a vessel: and decocted over fire, until the medicine turns red: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take what you need from it: and dissolve it with water and honey, and purify the sinus with it: and bind its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the duration of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure: do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the odor is removed.
And sometimes it is purified with ashes water when this medicine is not available to you: and it is that you take the ashes of vine wood or ashes from holm oak wood: and pour water over it. Then boil it and purify with it until you trust that the sinus is cleansed.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor ashes water, then purify it with water and honey, to which some ground ziniar is already mixed. Or cleanse it with honey and wine mixed together, for it is the property of honey to purify and wash clean: and wine makes the sinew adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do this several times and are confident that the sinew corruption has been removed, then cleanse it with something that promotes flesh growth.
And it is as follows: take some of the dyafinicon plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or cleanse it with certain medicines and other ointments whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
If the sinew orifice is narrow, through which the clyster tube cannot fit: then enlarge it temporarily with iron: or place lint wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment on it until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the orifice is also wide, then bring the edges together with a suture: and leave enough space for the clyster to enter without adding more.
And similarly, if the orifice from which pus runs is elevated towards the upper parts: then cut it at a lower place within it so that the pus may run down from it: for when pus is compressed in the depth of the sinew, it prevents flesh from generating there.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinew around its lower part as you wish: then try to prepare the member in such a way that pus may run out easily according to how it is prepared for you. Strive so that no pus at all remains in it: and take medicines for consolidation which are placed on the wound, suitable for what you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with the scissors straight at the opening of the sinus hole, somewhat larger than the opening of the sinus. Then spread a mild ointment over another cloth according to the size of the hole: and place it on it at a distance from the opening of the sinus, so that the discharge of pus from it is relieved: and do not remove the large cloth unless after several days.
The one that is on the opening of the sinus is the one you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus may come out: and to know whether what runs out of it as virus is much or little: or digested or undigested.
And with this, examine again the site of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or there is no pain in it, and whether there is a swelling in it or there is no swelling in it.
When therefore your treatment is extended thus, and you see the material that comes out over the opening of the sinus, then you know that the sinus has adhered or is approaching adhesion: then dissolve it in the morning or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change to another cloth and renew the ointment.
If, however, thin virus comes out of the sinus after a long period, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are people who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and among them are those who act contrary to this.
If, however, undigested pus exits from the sinus after many days, then you know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its condition is prolonged: then add to the drying medicines, but let the physicians make the medicines so that they are moist in their substance and dry in their power: such as diafrenicon ointment, when an excess of addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the latest method of treatment, it is to take diafrenicon ointment, and dissolve it with rose oil, and spray over it equal wine in its substance.
Then bind it with it, and use it.
And now it makes a similar action to this when you do not have diafrenicon ointment, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind all of it, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then apply it on the cloth, and bind it over the sinus: and apply with honey which you boiled until it thickened: and powder over it the spices, and place it on the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes administered in it is aristolochia longa, and roots of the celestial lily, and it is a lily: and flour of the herb, and centaury. Use them singly or in combination, grind them, and sieve them: and powder over the honey I mentioned, and use it.
If the condition of the sinus is prolonged and does not heal with this treatment: then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it already makes an impression on the bones, or on other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment for fistulas, according to what has been mentioned.
On the treatment of panaritium and ingrown nails, and the incision of the附加的指部，和切割。
Panaritium is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot: and it may also arise in the other digits.
When therefore the condition is prolonged and neglected by its caregiver, and it becomes abscessed with a hot abscess: or it corrupts and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and it may corrupt it entirely, and the corruption may reach the bone, until there is a foul odor: and the extremity of the digit becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then you must cut away with iron all the excess that remains of the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cauterization is very beneficial.
If however the bone is sound, and the nail is also sound, and the additions of the nail already compress the flesh towards the interior, and begin to pierce it, and hurt it: then you must place a thin probe under the angle of the nail that pierces the flesh, and push it up: and cut that flesh with ease, and apply over what remains of the flesh some burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If however the corruption has already set into the bone, then you must cut out that bone and remove it: for it will never heal as long as the corrupted bone remains.
If you see corrosion and decay spreading to the finger, then cut it at one joint, according to what has been previously mentioned. Then care for it until it heals.
If the nail is worn down or struck, and severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse section from top to view.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the patient by doing so, and it will result in additional flesh growing in that place.
Then treat the area with something that relieves pain.
The additional finger that is born on some people's hands may be entirely fleshy: and some of them may have bones, and perhaps a nail is in it: and some of them are born at the base of the joint, or its birth is in some joints of the finger. The one that is born on some joints of the finger does not move: and the one that is born near the joint of the finger may move.
The cutting of the one that is fleshy among them is easy. Which means you should cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
The treatment of the one whose birth is at the base of the joint is difficult. Therefore abstain from cutting it.
In the one who is born on a finger at the last joint, then it is necessary to first cut the flesh with a circular incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then care for it until it heals.
A consolidation which occurs in the fingers firmly, often happens either from the birth of the person, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from burning by fire, and similar causes.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them cloths, or fabrics soaked in rose oil: so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them; or place between them a thin lead plate until the area consolidates as it should.
And similarly, if a consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation according to how it is prepared and suits the shape of the member.
Regarding the incision for Vitium.
Warts are twisted veins, thick, filled with melancholic humors which occur in many parts of the body: and they most frequently appear on the legs, especially on the legs of travelers and laborers, and those who carry loads.
Therefore, you must administer shaking of the body from black bile several times with strong shaking.
Then apply the phlebotomy to the patient's basilica vein.
The cure of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is to cut and extract the black blood. And the other method is to raise the vein and extract it entirely.
The cutting of it is according to this method. Wash the leg first with hot water abundantly until the thick, feculent blood is dissolved. Then bind the leg of the patient from the upper part of the thigh down to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, extract the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg up, and it is necessary to phlebotomize the patient first from the upper part. Then cut down to the nail: until the amount of blood that you see is sufficient and which the patient's strength can tolerate comes out.
Then bind it and instruct him to abstain from foods that produce black bile. Repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are full and it is harmful to the patient.
Its elevation is according to this method. Bind the sick person's leg if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce him to the bath: or anoint him on his leg with warm water until it turns red, and the veins swell; or exercise him with strenuous exercise, if a bath is not available to him, until the limb becomes warm.
Then cut the skin directly along the vein's length: either at its end near the knee, or in the lower part of it near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears to the sense: and it is at its appearance, which you see it turn from red to a blackish color. When it comes out of the skin, you see it white like a string.
Then insert a probe under it until it is raised and comes out of the skin: suspend it with a blunt hook gently.
Then make another cut near that cut, about three fingers away. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another cut, or multiple cuts, if needed. Then raise it and cut it at the final cut near the heel.
Then extract it for the section which is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the upper section, or higher than all sections, until when it exits entirely: then cut it.
If it does not obey you for extracting and elevating: then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and extract it: or insert under it a lever: and revolve your hand in all directions until it exits. And beware that it is not cut. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to elevate it, and great harm enters the patient from it.
Therefore, when you have extracted it entirely: then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, on the wound site: and care for it until it heals.
If the vein is contracted, having a curvature that coils to parts, and is not straight as we have said: then it is necessary to incise along all parts of its curvature, and at the points where it coils.
Then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely: and beware at your cut on it that you do not cut the vein or wound it: for it will be difficult for you to elevate or extract it. Therefore, keep your caution.
Form of the Incision by Which the Vein is Healed.
Form of the Blind Hook.
It is not curved like other hooks, nor does it have a sharp end so as not to wound the vein: and it has a thick flexibility, gentle: for if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness: rather let there be an excess of thickness, as we said.
On the drawing of blood from the leg vein.
This vein is found in the legs in warm lands, such as Algeria, and the land of Arabs, and in dry, warm lands, lacking tenderness, that is, where no grass or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the putrefaction which occurs under the skin, like worms and ascarids generated within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this vein's event is that there occurs a violent inflammation in the leg. Then the area becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from that blistered area, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
When therefore its end appears, then you must wrap a piece of lead over it, whose weight is from one drachm to two drachms, and bind it to the knot itself: and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap that in lead, and bind it. If it is prolonged greatly, then cut part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before it comes out entirely. For if you cut, it contracts and goes into the flesh: and causes an abscess to occur, and rot in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it must be handled gently and extracted gradually until it comes out from the end of itself, and nothing remains of it in the body: and in some men, this vein comes out to a length of five palms and ten. And it has now come to me that it came out of a man for twenty palms.
If it is cut during your treatment: then insert a probe into its opening and pierce it with a long hole, along with the body, until all the matter inside is evacuated: and apply remedies to prevent rotting of the area for several days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle joint or on the foot itself: and it has many openings, and a branch comes out of each opening.
Treat it, therefore, as we have said in the division, and with what has been mentioned.
Regarding the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called Bouina disease.
This sickness is called in some land among us Bovine Sickness: therefore, because it often happens to oxen, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it goes throughout the whole body, ascending and descending: and it is felt during its movement from one limb to another, until it breaks through the skin wherever it breaks out and exits.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. As worms and snakes occur, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the stomach.
And it is feared for its maliciousness. Because when it moves in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it opens in it, and exits, and destroys the eye: and this happens often.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except during its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to bind tightly above and below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it and extract it.
If it is deep in the flesh and you do not find it: then place a cautery with fire on the spot until it dies.
And what is more feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
And if you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then grasp the eyebrow above it with a good clamp. Then cut above it, and remove it.
And it is necessary for the sick person to purify their body with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humors.
And let him be cautious of foods that cause putrefaction.
Regarding the incision for the Nakir disease.
This disease is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs: then it shifts from one limb to another.
And I have indeed seen it myself, as I tell you. I was called to an ill woman in some villas. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the rope-like vein of the arm.
And after I stayed for one hour, I saw a swelling moving with pulsation, like a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder faster than it could be: so that it is like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain retreated from that place and fixed on the shoulder.
Then I waited for one hour, and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then he told me that he was turning throughout his whole body, as I saw.
I marveled at the speed of the change from one member to another. And indeed, before that, I had not seen this disease with my eyes in such a manner according to this way. However, I have seen many who find the pain changing from one member to another: and I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything else but that the woman was one of those who have dry bodies with exposed veins: hence this change in windiness is apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that they do not appear in this manner in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
Therefore, when you wish to treat it and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to your eye as we said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it until the constrained windiness exits: and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your own eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body, and with what expels windiness, and drives it out with foul pills, and pills of serpentaria, and similar remedies.
Regarding the extraction of arrowheads.
Arrows are differentiated according to their types, and according to the places into which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is as follows: some have large points, and small ones: and concave, and deaf. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those which have tongues. And among them are those which have barbs.
Those, however, that fall according to the members into which they fall, are according to two modes. For either the members are concave principal parts, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and those similar to them. When therefore it falls into any of these members, and signs of death, which I tell you about later, become apparent to you, then you must avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone, and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, are: for there occur from it violent foaming, blindness, dizziness, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasms, confusion of the mind, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is severed, and voice is destroyed. And a white moist substance like pus comes out from the wound site. And there exits from it something resembling a watery substance of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple: and one feels with it as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty space. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the movement of a pulse: and black blood runs from the wound. And following that is the coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death comes undoubtedly.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the exit of foamy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes. And he exhales a great, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow falls into the membrane that is in the chest: then it is near the ribs: and a great breathing with intense pain occurs with sighing: and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps some undigested food comes out from the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach and is fixed in it: some waste comes out from the wound, or gall, or intestine which is already torn. Therefore, there is no hope for its cure, nor for the extraction of the arrow.
If an arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out: and something from it proceeds to the outer parts, and the pain is stronger on the patient: then you know that it is mortal.
The other members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and helper, and spine of the back, and collarbone, and thigh, and shin, and similar parts are treated according to many arrangements: when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve: and the arrow is not venomous.
And I tell you what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may signify through it concerning your treatment.
This is because an arrow fell into a man's tear duct, at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from the other side, beneath the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted an arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I could only reach a small part of it, with which it adheres to its wood: and it was a large arrow among the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
The Jew was healed, and no harmful accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the Christian's throat.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the one that has ears. I sewed over it between two fingers: and it was already submerged in his throat. I therefore maneuvered carefully with it until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his abdomen: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I sewed over the arrow: and I worked on it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no harmful accident happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had healed over it. After seven years had passed, the arrow emerged at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen into her abdomen, and the wound had healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen on his face: and the wound had healed, and it remained, not causing him much pain: and there are many similar cases to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man who was one of the chief men of the King, which had fallen to the middle of his nose, and it had already inclined slightly to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
I was called to his treatment three days after the arrow struck him. I found therefore the arrow wound very tight.
I examined it with a fine probe, and did not feel it: but he himself felt a sting and pain near his right ear. I suspected, therefore, that it was a pricking from the tip of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the area containing attractive and digestive properties, confident that the spot would suppurate and reveal a sign of the arrow so I could cut above it: but no occurrence happened in the place indicating that the arrow had reached its location.
I attended to it with a plaster for many days: and no incident occurred, and the wound healed over time: and he remained weak from its extraction for a period of days, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. And he reported that to me.
And he reported that to me. I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days until it opened: and I probed it with a probe, and felt the fine tip of the arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I continued to apply the sharp medicine to the wound opening until the tip of the arrow became visibly apparent: and I spent with him a period of time about four months.
Then after the wound was enlarged and it became possible for me to introduce forceps into it and move it, and it did not obey to come out: then I did not cease to refine and devise upon it with methods of ingenuity until one day I succeeded with properly made forceps, according to which their form will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians do judge that the cartilage of the nose does not restore itself. I therefore restored it, and the wound was healed, and the illness was cured with complete health; nor did anything harm him at all.
And I tell you the nature of extracting certain arrows: that you may set it as a rule and interpretation on what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended in the narration: nor does the book comprehend it, and a wise craftsman compares little with much: and what is present with what is absent: and finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descents of foreign matters of this art when they occur.
I say therefore that arrows do not exit from the members in which they are fixed, except by two methods. Or by attraction from the place whence it entered. Or to the opposite part.
And that which exits where it entered is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is attracted and exits.
If it does not obey and exit from the time when it falls: then you must leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it becomes putrid: and its attraction is lessened, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in the bone and does not obey you to exit, then leave it for several days: and iterate it with attraction and motion every day until it exits.
If it does not obey you to exit after days, then you must pierce around the arrow in the bone itself on all sides with a fine drill, until the arrow is loosened. Then pull it, and extract it.
If the arrow is already hidden in a part of the body and is concealed and hidden from perception: then seek it with probes. If however you feel it, then draw it out with some instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
If however you cannot reach it, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow being deep inside: and there is no opening, nor nerve, nor vein: then incise over it until the wound enlarges, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If it has ears by which it can be held, then free the flesh fixed to them on all sides with every skill you can apply, and use various methods if you cannot release the flesh due to the breaking of the ears and their twisting until it comes out.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with scissors to all sides, until you make it come out: and be very precise in the finest detail, so that the arrow does not break, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot reach it at once, then leave it for several days until what is around it becomes putrid. Then attempt it again: for the skin will be alleviated. If however the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we have said about the treatment in its chapter: and beware that your action does not lead to cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And employ every skill possible for the extraction of the arrow: and let it be done with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, the patient be positioned according to the shape in which they were at the time of the arrow's impact. For it is more beneficial. If you cannot do that, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
The arrow which exits from the opposite part of the other side, or already proceeds from it to the outer areas: or the tip of the arrow makes a bulge through the skin of the nearby upper part, and you see it protruding: therefore cut above it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grasp in it.
Then pull it out: for it becomes easy to remove.
If it is retained in the bone, then rotate your hand around the circumference until the arrow impresses itself into the bone and enlarges its own path: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for several days: then try it again until it comes out.
If the arrow is made of wood, then push it out with it.
If the wood has broken off and you wish to apply pressure: then insert a concave instrument into it so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow: then push it out with it.
If the arrow is concave, then push it out with an instrument inserted into it. For the arrow is lightened by that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut out all the flesh where the poison is: if you can do that. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is close enough to be touched with a probe, and you are able to cut over it: then cut, and beware of cutting into a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to a bird's beak, which are made like blades: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And various types are made, large, small, and medium, according to the size of the arrow's magnitude, its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its tightness.
The form of the concave pusher.
And the form of the deaf pusher.
This is the end of the deaf instrument like a rod, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased, and it can be pushed with it.
About the bloodletting of veins.
The veins to be opened, whose custom has run through the body, are thirty.
Of these, in the head there are sixteen: two Pulsating Veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs: and two Arteries, which are visible on both temples: and two Veins which are in the tear ducts of both eyes, which are called Aspicientes: and a Prepared Vein in the middle of the forehead: and the vein which is at the tip of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins which are opened in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is on the outer side: and the common people call it the head vein. And the Brown one. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the body vein. And the Basilic, and it is located on the inner side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, which is placed above the elbow: and it is the one that is bled from, and it is the one that appears above the thumb clearly visible.
And Salvatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is the one that is beneath the curvature of the thumb on the ulnar side. And the second is the Saphena: and its location is near the heel on the inner side. And the vein Nesse: and its location is near the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
About the bloodletting of two veins behind the ears.
The bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, hemiplegia, epilepsy, and old bad sores of the head.
And the method of bloodletting from it is as I describe. And it is done by shaving the head of the patient: and rubbing their occipital area where the two veins are with a coarse cloth thoroughly. Then the patient should constrict their neck with a ligature until the two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears in two depressed areas of the head. So seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with encaustic.
Then take the blade scalpel, and that is called the Incider. Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin using an incising cut. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or about that; and let as much blood flow as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book, the incision of them and their cauterization have been discussed.
The benefit of the phlebotomy of the two arteries which are in the two temples is for ancient hemigranea: and for sodam prauam, and persistent ophthalmia: and for the course of sharp humors flowing to the eyes.
And the method of phlebotomy for them is as I narrate to you. Let the patient tighten his neck greatly until two veins appear clearly to the sight: and their pulse is felt under your finger. Then indeed with signs with encaustic.
Then lift the skin over the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the flebotomy knife underneath, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it as you did with the other two veins: and let the blood flow according to the quantity necessary for your need.
Then release the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Then place a poultice and powder on it, and bind it tightly with a strong constriction: and leave it until it heals.
And already the recall of them, and their cutting, and their extraction at the beginning of the book has preceded.
The benefit of the flebotomy of the frontal vein after the flebotomy of the cephalic vein is for chronic facial illnesses: and for hemipity, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the nature of its flebotomy is as I narrate to you. Let the patient constrict his neck with a cord until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called the Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium on the vein, and strike it with a comb or with another instrument in its manner: and let the blood flow according to the quantity you desire. Then heal the perforation of the patient, and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide lancet. However, it should not have a sharp end like other lancets, but rather a broad end. And cut it with the lancet transversely: because the mouth is nearby: and perhaps the lancet may break there when it is thin.
The benefit of the two veins which are near the two tear ducts of both eyes is for the eye from scab and redness: and gravel in the eye, and ailments of the face.
And the method of bloodletting from them is that the patient should compress his neck with a band. Then lancet them: and you shall stand above his head.
And the bloodletting should be done transversely to a small length with a small, somewhat broad lancet, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the lancet has a thin end, it may break.
Then let as much blood flow as you deem necessary. And place a bandage over them, and bind them for one night, then release them.
Bloodletting from the nasal veins is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache, and facial ailments such as hemangioma, and the redness that occurs on the face: especially when it is old.
And the quality of its bloodletting is such that the patient should compress his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin, long bloodletting instrument, and insert it in the middle of the nasal septum, between the parts that divide the nose straight. Because the vein does not appear there to the sense, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you quickly withdraw your hand with the bloodletting instrument: and let the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it for one night. For it is restored quickly.
The benefit of bloodletting from the two Guidegi veins is for constricted breathing, early stages of leprosy, melancholic ailments, impetigo, and bad sores, and corrosive ulcers, that is, snake-like herpes.
And the quality of bloodletting from them is such that the patient should compress under both sides of his neck with a bandage: and the practitioner should stand above the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a seat.
Then the vein for bleeding should be broadly prepared to the length of the bleed. Then extract a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see is necessary for that case. Then do the same for another vein.
Then loosen the ligature, and bind the two veins with a moderate constriction: so that the patient is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Regarding the bloodletting of veins by Albucasis. The bloodletting of veins by Albucasis is beneficial: because it helps with alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad sores: and cracks in the lips, and bad ulcers, even those which are in the nose, and around it.
And the method of bloodletting is to have the patient sit in front of you: and bind his neck with a cord. Then turn his lip and look for two veins: one is on the right side of the lip, and the second on the left side: and they are identified by their darkness. And this is true: because around them are small, black veins. Cut both with a penetrating incision.
If there is doubt and you do not know which one to choose: then aim for the larger and more noticeable of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed the habit runs very much with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
On bloodletting The vein under the tongue after cephalic bloodletting. The benefit of bloodletting from the two veins under the tongue is that cephalic bloodletting is for obstructions in the throat, and sickness of the uvula, and illnesses of the mouth.
And the manner of bloodletting from them is that the patient should sit before you facing the sun: and lift his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein: and the color of both tends towards blackness.
Bloodlet from both veins: and be careful not to hesitate when bloodletting from them. For beneath them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood from those arteries may occur.
The three veins that are bloodlet from in the elbow are those from which bloodletting has become customary in all people.
And their bloodletting is done in two ways. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle lancet: or by using an olive-shaped fine lancet: or by cutting with a knife-like lancet, and this is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are the broad and thin types according to the width of the veins and their tightness. The form of the myrtle phlebotomy is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave veins, full, protruding, visible, thick, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy is of lesser width and finer at the extremities.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of thin veins which contain fine yellow blood.
But the basilica, which is one of these three veins, is beneficial for phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses that are under the throat and neck, from what follows the chest, and the abdomen: and it is suitable for the phlebotomist to be cautious when phlebotomizing it, to be in fear of it, for an artery is beneath it. If he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will hit that artery, and a flow of blood will result. Therefore, it is necessary that he not puncture her with the phlebotomy; rather, he should cut with Alnesil.
If the basilic vein does not appear clearly visible, then you must move away from it and switch to another, or seek some branch of it; or bloodlet at its place in the arm. For it is clear, and cut it with the bloodletter Anesil, as we said.
If you wish to bloodlet from it, then before tightening the arm, you must touch the place until you recognize the pulse location. Then mark the place with cautery, then bind the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the bloodletter Alnesil, as we said. And be careful that the incision does not fall away from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the band, swelling at the place you marked with cautery, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery: avoid it.
If you see the blood at the bloodletting leap like a boy's urine, and it is fine, red blood: then you know that it is from the artery's blood. Then hurry, and place your finger on it for a long space, then remove your finger. If the blood is interrupted (for it is often interrupted), then bind the arm and release it: and let the patient avoid moving it: and let it be covered with fear.
And let him not move it for several days until it is healed.
If however the blood is not severed and overcomes you: and your medicine is not present at your moment then cut the artery if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and the blood is severed.
Or take the bark of the fig, and dry it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein's constriction properly, with a bandage and poultices until the next day.
Then if the blood is severed, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that which precedes the memory from the place of powders severing the flow of blood: and the severing of its blood is not difficult in most conditions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of binding from the arm.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the Fuscae vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body: for this reason, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica, as we have said.
And it is appropriate for the phlebotomist to be fearful of it, because there is a nerve beneath it. If therefore the phlebotomist adds pressure at the puncture and finds a nerve, numbness occurs in it, healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not fully healed.
And this nerve often appears to the sense. If however it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and is not shown: then you must place your vein-opening with which you open the vein, a cut with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein along its length.
About the cephalic vein. Opening the cephalic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and helps with eye diseases.
And you must properly in this vein, if you wish, open it by pricking with an olive lancet or with a myrtle lancet: for it is safer than all other veins, for there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, it is fitting for you when opening veins, to avoid the armpit with the lancet and seek a softer spot: and it does no harm if it is not found on the first attempt, so that it may be tried over again with the lancet several times.
However, it may possibly swell in some men when it is not opened on the first attempt, but that swelling does no harm.
About the usefulness of bloodletting and the precautions to be observed in it. The nature of bloodletting and its effects must be considered by you for its proper execution.
Therefore, the first thing you should know is that bloodletting is not administered except for the preservation of health, its continuance, and as a precaution against the onset of illnesses.
And so that bloodletting may be done on one of three veins which are in the elbow, namely Cephalica, and Fusca, and Basilica. And so that bloodletting may be done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in spring and summer: and in autumn, and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And bloodletting shall be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed since the day began.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses does not have a fixed time: but when circumstances and necessity call for it, whether at night or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
And it should not be that children be bled until they come to fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should seniors be bled who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason: it is necessary that they cleanse their intestines before bloodletting with a mild enema, if there is much retained feces in them: so that the veins around the bloodletting do not attract putrid and harmful superfluities from the intestines to the principal members.
Nor should a patient be bled who is suffering from toctina, that is, nausea-inducing fullness: nor when drunken, nor when recovering from intoxication, until these conditions are removed from them. Neither the patient with dropsy.
And let bloodletting be avoided after colic pain, and vomiting, and flux of the bowels, and multiplication of coitus, and labor, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and everything which dissipates strength from the body or animal.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if possible, do that for him.
Then let him enter the bath, if nothing prohibits it: or let him be exercised in some exercise, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his bloodletting on the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said: and endeavor that his chest be evacuated that day from all harmful animal accidents: such as distress, anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, pain, and excess, and those similar to these.
And let there be present with his seat things with which human custom has run from their aromatics, and fragrances, and entertainments and similar things according to the extent of possibility.
Then let the bloodletter sit on a pillow that is higher than the pillow for bloodletting: afterwards, he should extend his arm and the bloodletter shall rub it twice or thrice with his hand. Then bind the ligature with a bandage, and wrap it twice.
And let the binding be moderate, for when the binding is unequal, whether by excess in tightness, then it impedes the flow of blood: or in looseness, then it also impedes the flow of blood.
Then after binding it is necessary that the one to be bled should rub his hands together vigorously until the veins swell and are made clear to the sense.
Then the phlebotomist should cleanse the vein with a little oil, in the old proper way.
Then he should place his left index finger on the vein itself at the spot where he wants the phlebotomy to be done, slightly: so that the vein does not flee, and the strike is successful. For the veins are like a fleeing string during phlebotomy: and there is one which is full of air: and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the lancet does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is constricted: therefore, the phlebotomist must be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should put down the lancet. If therefore the vein is opened at once, then it is good. And if not, then do it again at another spot nearby, or above it quickly: provided the area is not inflamed.
If it is inflamed, or the patient becomes swollen, then leave it for a day or two days: and do not bind the ligature. For it may draw the inflammation to a warm area. Nor should he enter the bath.
Then repeat bloodletting, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or blood flows thinly: and you fear that the quantity of blood you wish will not emerge: then repeat the bloodletting in the same hole straightly, and add a little to the procedure.
And do it with haste before the place becomes abscessed, for an abscessed place is in many people a minor operation.
If you see it already abscessed, then do not repeat anything on it at all, for nothing excuses it to you. And put something from the dregs of oil on it: for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your bloodletting, where you bloodlet veins to prohibit the flow of blood: and they do this with mithridate, alfarak, and sage, when one of them is placed on the spot.
For blood is thinned and dissolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the site of bloodletting, for it often happens, especially to one who is not blooded except that once, or the vein opening is small, then hurry and place on the area a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated slightly: and bind it for one hour: for it dissolves.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited from the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after days, it does not harm that: and if you wish, put over it something of dissolved aloe or myrrh: or something from the juice of menthastri, that is, calamus or similar to that.
And indeed, swellings and protrusions often occur around the phlebotomy at the Basilica.
Place therefore your hand upon it, then if you find softness at your compression upon it, then that swelling is bad. Beware therefore, to put upon it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood from an artery may flow from it.
But make a poultice for it with something that has astringency, so that the area may harden. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary to withdraw blood from every person according to the measure of their strength. And what appears from the dominating color over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it turns red: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it flow until it becomes thin: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and in need of bloodletting once, to enlarge the vein incision, and let the incision be broad: and for one who is weak, it should be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he pulls it out in places, and that the hole is narrow: and better than what is administered in bloodletting of veins is a transverse cut, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow, and from nerve cutting: and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than bloodletting through breadth and length.
And for one who is accustomed to faint at bloodletting, it is necessary that they eat before bloodletting a little bread soaked in pomegranate juice musli: or secaniabin, if it is heated: and you should draw from their blood in three places, or four.
And if it is of a chilled complexion, then let them take before bloodletting bread soaked in honey syrup, or in aromatic honey syrup with spices, or in aromatic fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and the cause is a large loss of blood: then it is necessary that they drink water with meat and subtle fragrant syrup: and use aromatics with algalia, and anoint their chest with it: and use the other things we mentioned in the division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
Who wishes to bleed his arm and release his blood a second time: it is necessary for the one who undergoes bloodletting because of much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release blood gradually according to the amount of strength over consecutive days.
Who wishes to bleed his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
Whoever wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite part from which it declines: it must happen to him on the second day, or on the third.
In whose body there is already a lot of heated blood, and it sharpens, and causes fever to occur: it must be that blood exits from him in one vein, and a great quantity is extracted from him: and the opening is enlarged until syncope occurs: after you have been preserving all the conditions of bloodletting: and placing your hand on his pulse at the course of the blood, so death does not occur instead of syncope. For that often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not appropriate when you want to release the arm, and release blood second time, after the vein's orifice is already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, to press on it with violence, and quickly extend it while bent. For that is very bad, indeed leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of the phlebotomist what has coagulated from blood in the vein's orifice, and place something dissolved salt in water on it. And place on it something from theriac alfarabi, or sagzenea, and place it with gentle compression until blood flows out.
If the vein has already become apstedematous, then leave it uncovered until the aposteme subsides.
If necessity provokes to release blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either apply phlebotomy above that place or in the other arm in another vein.
The rope of the arm is phlebotomized at the place of the Fuscae and the Basilicae, when they are not found and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the patient should insert his hand into warm water until the vein becomes red and appears clearly.
Then it should be bound above it with a bandage with slight constriction in the middle. Then phlebotomize gradually across, not widthwise, nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy of how you phlebotomize be at the joint of the hand for a moment.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then dip the hand in warm water again, and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your need.
If, however, you are in the summer days, then you are excused from dipping the hand in warm water again. And it is placed that the flow of blood in warm water occurs most frequently in winter.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
The phlebotomy of the Saluatellae of the right hand is beneficial for ailments of the liver.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should bind the vein of the hand with a ligature or your own hand after you introduce it into warm water until the vein swells and is very noticeable to the sense. Then puncture it transversely. And if you cut it entirely, it will not harm her in any way.
And be careful not to leave your hand with the phlebotomizer at the end. For beneath it the nerves of the fingers are exposed without flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will clot at the vein's opening and be prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly, you should do this with every tight branch.
The benefit of its phlebotomy from the left hand is that it is beneficial for spleen ailments; and similarly perform its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for ailments in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the wombs, retention of menses, infirmities of the kidneys, and old sores of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments.
And the quality of bloodletting is such that the sick person should insert his foot into warm water: and he should make friction upon it until the veins swell: and bind above the joint of the foot with a strap. And the place of the veins is near the heel, appearing towards the big toe: and many branches branch out from it on the surface of the foot.
Therefore, bloodletting should be done in the larger branch of it, or near the heel at its collection: for it is better and safer.
If you bloodlet from it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your bloodletting instrument across where you bloodlet it, as if you want to cut it: and the bloodletter should be Nesil.
If however the flow of blood is prevented, then soak his foot again in warm water: and allow the blood to flow into it until it is evacuated.
If the bloodletter misses the vein on the first attempt, then repeat the bloodletting slightly higher up, for the area is safe, and you need not fear any harm from it when you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the Saphena of the other foot equally.
The location of the Sciatic veins, as we have said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of bloodletting is for the pain of gout, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the patient should enter the bath and hurry: and bind his leg on the side of the anchor up to four digits above the heel with a thin long string: for it does not appear except for that reason.
When therefore it appears, then bloodlet from it according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to the crosswise manner: and this is better, or cut it with an incision, or cut it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it and it does not appear at all to the sense: then bloodlet from one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, towards the little toe: and be careful of the nerves, and let as much blood as you wish.
Then loosen the binding, and place skin over the site of bloodletting, and bind the area. For it heals quickly.
Regarding ventosation, and the quality of its use.
Wind instruments are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And ventosation is performed in two ways. With scarification and extraction of blood: the other is ventosation without scarification.
And this ventosation which is without scarification is performed in two ways. For it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And for windy conditions which are treated with scarification and bloodletting, there are fourteen places on the body. Of which one is Ventosatio Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and the wind vents Alhagdahim, and they are two plates on both sides of the neck simultaneously: and the wind vent of the chin, and it is below the jaw on the lower part of the mouth: and the wind vents of the two spatulas: and the wind vents Alachosos at the end of the tail: and the wind vents of the two focilium, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and the wind vents of the two thighs: and the wind vents of the two cavillarum.
And Ventosatio indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of this, strength does not fall from them, as is the case with bloodletting. Nor should ventosatio be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any diseases that arise from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
If necessity prompts ventosation due to illness or habit, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are people for whom when blood multiplies within them until they need its extraction through ventosation, they find heaviness and sodahan in their head.
And among them is one who finds fullness and redness on his face, head, and neck. And among them is one who finds itching on his face, and forehead, and darkness, and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the place of his windiness. And among them is one whose laughter multiplies. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or his tongue swells, and he spits out blood. And among them is one whose sleep multiplies. And among them is one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to those.
When therefore we see any of these things, and especially if it is on the third day of the month: then we prescribe wind expulsion to be done, after two or three hours have passed.
The benefit of the windiness of the neck is that it contributes to heaviness in the head and what flows to the eyes. But it should be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this Ventosa is in place of cephalic bloodletting.
And it is not permissible for one of a cold temperament to use it, or for one who has catarrh. For it causes him great harm.
And for that reason, the elderly should not use it, and those whose heads suffer from cold illnesses.
Whoever becomes accustomed to it, forgetfulness is generated upon him.
And for that reason, you should instruct the cupper to temporarily place his hand with the cup towards the lower parts, fearing the onset of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in place of black bloodletting and basilic bloodletting, and for that reason it benefits asthma, constriction of breathing, windpipe rupture, cough, and fullness.
And it should be that the cup of Alkehel is temporarily applied higher up, because if placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping with alhagdahim, however, benefits headaches, ophthalmia, migraines, piercing pains, and toothache: and it is a substitute for basilic bloodletting.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand with scarification so as not to hit an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Cupping under the chin benefits alcoholism in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar ailments that are in the mouth: and it serves as a substitute for bloodletting from the veins that are in both lips.
Cupping of the spatulas benefits alchafacham, which is caused by obstruction and heat.
Blowing on the inner parts of two flasks provides as much benefit as opening three veins with a lancet, the Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica: for it draws blood from all those tiny veins which are in the flesh: and these tiny veins draw from other thicker veins than themselves, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And you must instruct the Ventosator not to proceed too much in scarification: for the area is stripped of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The ventosa which is applied over alchausos provides relief to hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And you must instruct the Ventosator to use a large ventosa, and that it be of air: for the area requires strong suction, and perhaps delicate Ventosae may break: and it should be scarified with a large incision.
Ventosae of the legs produce a noticeable reduction: for they draw blood from the whole body, and alleviate old pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and help bothor and ademenul: and serve in place of opening two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they greatly weaken the body and cause syncope to occur in many people.
And the benefit of the alhorcobeni ventosae is similar to the benefit of the ventosae of the two legs.
The quality of the placement of ventosae is to first place the ventosa empty and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of the ventosae, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with an equal arrival: and do not stop iterating and continuing this until you see the place already redden and swell, and the redness of blood appear: then scarify and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For he who is of tender flesh and sparse pores, you must scarify him once, not more: so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the bloodletter to enlarge the scarification and make it a little deep: and moderate the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is thickness in the blood, then you must scarify him twice. In the first juice, the way is opened for the refinement of blood and its aqueous quality: and in the second, for the thorough extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very feculent, then let there be a third scarification: so as to achieve the ultimate.
And finally, when we want to extract blood gradually or for a short while, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we want to extract much blood, we must scarify with multiple scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it must be scarified with deep scarifications: and the limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is to be administered from oils at the site of bloodletting, and what the person being bled should beware of.
In the case of one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, the places for bloodletting should be anointed with oils that are opening, soothing, and dissolving: if it is in the summer season, such as oil of alkeiri, or violet oil, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the winter season, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or chamomile oil, or saffron: and things like these.
And if the excess is thick and cold: then the oil should be of rue, or oil of balsamite, or oil of alkanet, or oil of dill: and things like these.
And if the windiness is of large pores, tender flesh: then it should be prohibited from oil, and these places of windiness should be washed after windiness with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solatri, or with water of cucumber, or with water of portulaca: and things like these.
He whose blood is of much moisture, should wash windiness with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and things like these.
Whose excesses are thick, should wash his windiness with old wine, or with sage water, or dill decoction, or chamomile: and things like these.
And it should be guarded that windiness is administered in a bath, or near the bath, but rather it should be given after coming out of the bath by an hour, or by two hours.
And it should not be that someone sleeps after windiness.
What is it that should regulate windiness or bloodletting before windiness.
And after that, you should first consider: then if the windy or bloodletted person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then they should take cooling things, such as pomegranate marmalade: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and chicory, and rue: and things like these.
And place his foods as chickens, and meats of sheep sekebiget, and seasoned foods with herbs: and things similar to these.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take fragrant wine that is between old and new: and let it be ordered that he use sparingly of food. And place his food as chickens, and canebir: and sparrows, that is, small birds: and chickens of doves issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than food: and it is necessary that in drink there be given to some men at times theriaca alfaroch, or medicine of musk, that is, muscata, or alesselithe before cupping: and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal members are strengthened, and the blood is refined. And they should not take it in heated drink.
Regarding Windiness without Scarification. Windiness without scarification, however, is Windiness that is placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the stomach, navel, and areas of the kidneys, and the anchor's box: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And they are sought for only by the attraction of blood from one member to another: as we place Windiness upon the breasts in the illness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to dissolve cold indissoluble wind in the limb: just as we place wind on the belly and navel. For it rarefies the limb, and heats it, and removes pain because it dissolves that wind.
It is also placed on the kidneys when obstruction or stone occurs in them. For by the power of its attraction, it perhaps opens the obstruction or uproots the stone from its place.
And it does the same when placed on the liver and spleen for dissolving wind in both of them.
And these winds are administered empty with only cutting. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in pleurisy illnesses: and this is done by filling the ventosa with hot water alone, or water in which herbs that are suitable have been decocted.
The method of applying the ventosa with fire.
