Let the diameter of its opening be two fingers wide, according to how we have formed it: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole on its side at a place that is about the middle of it, according to the size through which a needle can enter: it should be made of copper or brass, thick-edged, smooth, equal, clean: so that it does not harm the limb when placed upon it: and let there be a transverse rod in its center made of metal or iron where a candle with fire is placed.
And now it becomes this large and hollow, larger than what we mentioned, and somewhat smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
For the bellows of children and those who are thin are different from the bellows of men and those with thick bodies.
The quality of placing this bellows with fire on the limb is to light a strip of linen with fire properly, or a small candle from wax: and place it in the middle of the crossbar which is in the middle of the bellows: so that the fire is lit around the lower part of the bellows, so that the body of the patient is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb: and a finger should be on the hole I mentioned, until when we hold the bellows: while we need to, remove the finger, and vapor will come out through that hole, and the bellows will immediately release.
Then ignite the strip of linen according to its manner, and repeat it, if you need to do so.
In the bellows which is used for the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no crossbar, nor a hole: and it is not used except that it is filled with water and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this bellows, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Concerning the hanging of bloodsuckers.
Leeches are not administered in many places, unless in those members where it is not possible to place cups, because of the smallness of them, such as the lip, and the gum, and things like them. Or on a member stripped of flesh, such as the finger, and nose, and things like them.
And the quality of using them is: to obtain leeches which are in sweet waters, clean from putrefactions.
Then let them be kept day and night in sweet water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their stomach. Then the body should be purged first with bloodletting and with cupping. Then clean the patient's limb until it turns red. Then place them on it.
Therefore, when they are filled and fall off, and it is possible to suction the area with a cupping vessel, then it is most beneficial; and if not, then wash the area with vinegar. Then with plenty of water, it should be rubbed and squeezed.
If the flow of blood continues after the leeches fall off, and it is seeping out, then soak a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the seepage stops.
If the blood flows excessively, then powder and sprinkle ground zego, or gallnuts, and things like them that are astringent, until the bleeding stops.
Or let halves of peeled beans be placed upon the site: and leave them until the beans adhere in place, for the blood is congealed.
And it is necessary if the repetition of bloodletting is required, when possible, to change the places.
If bloodletting is prohibited due to suspension, then the area should be cleaned with fresh blood. Or pierce with a needle in the places until some blood comes out. Then let them be placed, for they themselves when they feel some of the blood, adhere immediately.
When therefore you want them to fall off, sprinkle something over them from crushed aloe, or salt, or ash: and they will fall off immediately.
This Chapter is also frequently necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractured and dislocated bones.
Know, O Sons, that foolish doctors and the common folk boast about this Chapter, and those who have never examined an ancient book or read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is deprived from the sciences in our land. And I have never obeyed in that completely good thing, and I did not achieve from it what I achieved except through the length of reading ancient books, and studying their understanding, until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experience and study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you from that in this Chapter all that my knowledge contains: and beyond what my experience has passed, after I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the burden of lengthiness, and shortened it to the end of abbreviation, and showed it to the end of deviation: and I formed many shapes in it from the forms of instruments which are used in it, as it is with the addition of deviation, just as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
We must say in the prologue of this Chapter the summaries of the discourse and the chapters, to whose understanding the artisan is first compelled to stand upon their truth: namely, so that you and those who are eager may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall happens to someone, then you must hasten first to bleed him and to relieve him, or both together: unless something prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or the one to whom any of these happen is a child or an infirm old man; or the time is of vehement heat or extreme cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and flesh of birds, and of goats: and prohibit wine, and fatty meats, and to be filled with foods and all food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and you do not fear the discharge of matter to the limb: then let the sick person return to his original diet with which he was accustomed.
When therefore the fractured bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed nourishing foods with much, thick, heavy nutrition in which there is viscosity: such as food made from wheat cooked with water, and roots, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. With this regimen, the fracture is healed faster and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in the early old age, and in the infirm, cannot always be joined and consolidated according to their nature because of the dryness of their limbs: and they are consolidated and joined at the end of softening, like the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something similar to glue on all sides of the fractured bone, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and tightens itself, until they are united with each other, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary to provide the sick person's nourishment with foods that have heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And you shall know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull bone: and the fracture of the rib is different from the fracture of the back bone: and similarly all the other limbs exist differently from each other: and I come with the recollection of every type of fracture exposed in its own chapter distinguished from another. And the types of bone fractures also differ among themselves.
For its fracture is arched, besides splinters occurring in it. And sometimes its fracture is along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintered, that is, splinters, and projections proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is cut with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture, a small fissure.
And every type has its own method or instrument for its restoration, according to which its recollection will come, distinguished in their places.
And by which the fracture of the bone is recognized is its twisting and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor sound at your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is a slight twist or small fissure: therefore it is not necessary to move it with hand and compression at all, but instead apply some of the medicines whose recollection comes, after the appropriate moment for the place: then bind it with a fine bandage.
And you shall know that when the place is curved and split into two, besides the pus that may occur in it, nevertheless all parts are removed from their companion: then it is necessary for you to begin at your appointed time for its correction, equalizing it before warm pus befalls it.
And if warm pus befalls it, then leave it for several days until the pus quiets down. Then equalize it according to whichever method can be done based on its tenderness and nature.
And you shall know that the restoration and equalization of it is easier than that of bone in which pus occurs.
Then bind it according to how the memory of its treatment comes to you.
If pus is in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from both parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand if the limb is small: or with two ropes, or with one rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an equal ground according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then endeavor with all ingenuity and in whatever way you can to return those additions to their places: and beware according to your strength, lest excessive pain or injury occur through your operation: and endeavor according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its companion in the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time for you to test and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the extent of your skill.
And beware of strong extension and forceful compression, as many foolish ones do. For often they cause by their operation a hot swelling or contraction in the limb: as we have already seen many times from their operations.
Then apply after equalization, and proper treatment, and binding of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and advise the patient about it. And make him beware not to move it during his waking hours, and in his sleep regarding his turning, and stirring, and in his excretion, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And let him beware that the position of the limb does not cause pain. Which means that when he feels pain or injury in the positioning of the limb: then it is necessary to change it to another position where he does not feel pain.
And beware that its position is equal and straight: so that no curvature occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the binding of the fractured limb is as I narrate.
Know that fractured limbs differ in their smallness, and their magnitude, and their form.
Therefore, those among them that are small, such as the arm, and finger, and shin, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of a soft, fine cloth. Which of them are thick, like the thigh and leg, should have a wide, hard wrapping.
For the wide bandage connects the large limb and tightens it on all sides with equal constriction, so that no spot enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation to apply an appropriate liniment to that, on mild tow over the site of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture itself with three or four wrappings, according to what the limb deserves: and hold your hand firmly with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the site of the fracture, and hold less tightly than your previous constriction with which you constricted the fractured place. Then move away with the wrapping from the site of the fracture gradually, and loosen the constriction little by little until you reach a large part from the healthy area.
Then take another strip and wrap it again over the fractured area with more wrappings. Then proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the constriction of the wrapping and its loosening, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings with mild tow or cloth, with which the curvature of the fracture is matched, if there is curvature in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Then apply evenly over these properly made bandages, immediately if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If there is swelling or an abscess, then place on it where the abscess sits to reduce the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then bind the bandages over it tightly.
And the bandages should be made from the halves of a wide, shaved, and wisely shaped cane; or they may be made from wood used for sieves, which are made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and the width of each bandage, according to this form, should have the same shape.
However, the bandage that is placed over the traction should be thicker and somewhat wider than the other bandages: and the length of the bandage should be according to what is suitable for the member in size and smallness.
Then bind over the bandages with another strip of cloth according to the same tightness as the first. Then bind over it with threads appropriately, according to what we have said about tightness: and it is so that your binding over the fracture site should be tighter; and the farther away from the fracture it is, the looser the binding should be.
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be made of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the work of foolish ones who use twisted linen chords, it is a great mistake: for the tightness with them falls outside the proper quality.
And fine threads again do not suit: because you do not achieve with them what you want through tension.
And it should not be less than a finger between the peg and the peg.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the pegs after tightening at healthy sites: then place under them soft tow or carded wool until nothing is injured from that.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come to it with a recollection of its singularity in the second chapter.
And you should know that not every fractured limb must be bound with pegs on the first day. This is because when the limb is large, you should not place pegs on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the risk of hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored fractures and dislocations.
The method of a common abbreviated plaster that restores fractures, suitable for most complexions, especially women and children: because it is such that heat does not overcome it nor cold.
And it is done by taking the millstone dust, which is the flour powder that hangs on the walls of the mill near the motion of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white: and set its consistency not thick nor thin. Then use it.
Method of another plaster for healing fractures, dislocations, and torsions. Take amber, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and the juice of all ʒ X myrrh, and aloes both ʒ V and alcohol ʒ XX are to be ground, sifted, and mixed with alcohol: or with egg white, if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, heals broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Method of another plaster which also benefits fractures and torsions. Take mugwort, and moss, and white hollyhock, of each ʒ X myrrh, and aloes both ʒ V acacia ʒ VI Armenian bole ʒ XX grind all thoroughly, sift, and prepare with water, or egg white: and administer.
Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and it alleviates the pain occurring therein, and bone cutting, and fracture. Take suet wool, and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil: and place it on the area.
And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good for soothing a hot abscess: and it properly relieves pain.
The method of the plaster which restores the broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree and the leaves of the wild poppy; and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
The method of the shortened plaster, applied for the restoration of a fractured bone: and when you wish for the resolution of remaining abscess. Take the root of althea, and chamomile, and flowers of viola, and flour of the herb, of all one part each; grind all, and prepare with wine, if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then prepare it with humid coriander water, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs at the restoration of a limb. Take the root of althea, and seeds of flax, and pomegranate blossoms, and sweet clover, and marshmallow, and flowers of viola, and chamomile, of all one part each; grind all, and prepare with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. Use the whole according to the heat of the member, and the subsidence of its fever.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except plaster made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he described it to be of a middle consistency between thickness and fineness.
Galenus thought that over the fractured limb during its restoration should be placed things which have a drying heat, such as myrrh, and aloe and frankincense, and similar to them from those which we have narrated.
Method of the plaster that helps with the injury and pain. Take mugwort, and chickpeas, and human hair cuttings, or bird feathers, and coltsfoot, and salt in equal parts: they are to be ground, sieved, and prepared: and a plaster is to be made from it.
The quantity according to which it is necessary for the bandage to remain, then be loosened, is such that you should consider whether no pain, nor itching, nor movement of the fractured bone from its place has occurred: then do not loosen it for many days. If a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the place: then hurry and loosen it within an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides.
If a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the place: then hurry and loosen it within an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, on it: and bandage it for one night, until you are sure there is no hot swelling, and the inflammation of the limb subsides, and its pain is relieved.
Then adjust it to a fine binding, and a small plaster: and do not bind it tightly with your first bandage, and keep it fine until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and binding, then treat it again as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we have mentioned happens to the member, then do not loosen it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, do everything according to what appears to you from loosening the member as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approximates over it: then also add more to the binding than your whole first binding: and also increase the infirm man's food according to his previous recollection of it.
If you see the place of the fracture already drying out and becoming thinner than it should be: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, wash it with warm water every time you loosen it, and do this for all three days, and lighten the binding for a while.
Through this operation, nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do with a fractured member by using another method when it is not restored properly at first and is restored crookedly, is an error from their operation, and a great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and operated accordingly.
And I did not see any trace of them doing that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
On the treatment of head injuries.
The types of fractures are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture caused by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely until it reaches the saphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does to wood: and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts through part of the bone, and cuts its surface, but the cut does not penetrate to the end: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Absolute Elevation.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is grinding or wearing: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which approaches the membrane that is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or large.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone like a hair's subtlety: and it is a small crack, and because of this, this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And among them is a fracture that occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the bone layer to intrude inward, and a concavity is formed: like what happens to bellows when they are struck and part of them intrudes inward.
And it happens mostly in the heads of humid bones, as are the heads of children.
And separate efforts are made for all these kinds of fractures, and I will come with a recollection of the whole of it in its proper place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by examining them, and probing them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh that is on them.
The capillary type is known by this, that it is revealed on the bone, and is cleaned, and anointed on it with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient: then if you see from the symptoms that it indicates clear signs of fear, such as choleric vomiting, and extension, and loss of reason, and cessation of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of eyes, and redness of them, and similar ones among the symptoms in most dispositions, doubtless it is not cured: and if you see symptoms that do not frighten you, and hope for recovery: then begin his treatment.
Which means, if a wounded person comes to you when they are first wounded: and this is during the winter months, then it is necessary that you focus on the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all dispositions.
And if it is during the summer months, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the membrane becomes corrupted, and those symptoms we have mentioned occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with fragments, and there is compression: then it is necessary that you cut away the crushed and worn part, as I tell you.
And it is as if you shave the head of the wounded: and expose over the bone in whatever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light over the injured area.
If during the exposure of the bone there happens to be a flow of blood or a warm abscess: then apply what is necessary to it. And it is as if you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with an instrument of a sharp edge: and this is its form.
Then apply another cutter after the narrower one for a moment: and this is its form.
Then apply yet another cutter wider than the second one.
And the second method is to have a number of different cutters with you: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are shorter than others: and they should be sharp at their ends, and they should be made of iron or steel: and use them easily in striking on the cutter so that it does not shake the head and harm it.
If the bone is hard and strong: then you must pierce around it before you apply the cutting instruments with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are not so named unless because they do not pass through the limit of the bony flesh to that which is behind it: therefore, the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a great number, each of which suits the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill suitable for every skull: the sharpness of whose ends in length and brevity should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of drills, or trephines.
The quality of piercing around the fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place it so that there is the thickness of a rod or about that amount between every hole.
Then cut with the cutting instruments what is between every hole in the bone: and make it finer than you can on the lighter end, as we said, until you root out the bone, either by hand or with some other of the instruments you have prepared for this: such as forceps and delicate chisels.
And you must be cautious with all care that the drill or cutting instrument does not touch anything from the sinus.
When therefore you circle around the bone perforating, and it is separated from the siphac, if it was continuous with it, and you extract it, you must scrape, and level the end of what remains in the bone with another similar cutting instrument. However, it must be thinner and finer than other cutting instruments.
If something remains of the small bones and splinters, then remove them easily with what you have among your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with lint and the ointments we have mentioned.
And another method of the operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen mentioned and praised highly: and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and evident: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the end of this lens-shaped cutting instrument, which has this form.
And the lens-shaped part, being blunt, does not cut anything: and the sharp part of it running along the two sides in length, as you see: so that the lens-shaped part is placed against the splinter, with its sharp face facing the bone.
Then strike on the cutting instrument from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns: and you shall be secure about the membrane, that no accident happens to it which you fear entirely.
And if the medic were not stupider than others and more ignorant of it, nor were asleep, then if anything of the membrane remained attached to the bone in certain places, he would cut it off with the end of the lens-shaped cutting instrument and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculus, and that which is severed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine projections remain in it: then you must scrape away that roughness, and root out those projections with subtle razors of various sizes that you have prepared in number and diversity, so that you may be able to use those that are more suitable and truer for each place according to what the operation leads you to do, and also the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And you must act according to your judgment when you first scrape the bone, using the wider of those razors. Then use the finer one after it.
And you must act according to your judgment when you first scrape the bone, using the wider of those razors. Then use the finer one after it. And do not cease to do this continuously until you reach the point of using the finest and most subtle of them all.
In each of the remaining small capillary fissures and fine fractures, you must administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And this is something that is not hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and who has stood over what we have written and shown in major fractures.
But if the panniculus detected is retained when you remove the bone: then you must take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and make it as light as you can on top of it so that it is not too heavy.
Then apply a broad bandage over it: and do not bind it tightly, except to the extent that it holds the wound: and leave it with this care for one day or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and apply after it medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and flour of the herb, and incense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is that you make from these medicines a powder, which is dusted over the wound like they are dry.
And universally apply to it all medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to irritate. And be careful according to your ability, that the wound in your care is clear: neither let there be any grease filth in it, nor impurities.
And be careful according to your ability, that the wound in your care is clear: neither let there be any grease filth in it, nor impurities. And do not allow pus to gather in it at all: for when pus gathers on the brain membrane, it corrupts and putrefies it, and great harm befalls the patient thereafter: and it often happens in headaches, when the bone is cleansed of it, especially when its care is neglected, a blackening appears on its surface.
Therefore, consider then, if the patient experiences the conditions we have mentioned, you shall know that it is certainly perilous.
And if blackening does not occur except from the medicine placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackening: then you must take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and line the cloth with them. Then place him on a shiffin: afterwards care for him with the kinds of treatment which are suitable, until he is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is fractured.
You should know that only the two upper parts of the nose, or one of them, can be fractured; because there are two bones in it: since its lower part is made of cartilage, it does not fracture, and what happens to it is only wear, distortion, and flattening.
If one of the two parts is fractured, then you must insert a small finger into the nostril and align that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape: and this should be done skillfully, with ease. And beware that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be aligned using the tip of a probe that has a small thickness.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do the same: and hurry with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the inflammation has subsided.
Then insert a strip of linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part: or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts: and let the strip have a thickness sufficient to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that licinia should be infused into butter, and it should be changed every day. That indeed does not seem right to me; rather, it is necessary that licinium be infused into the albumen of an egg, mixed with millstone powder. Then leave the licinium until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes, instead of licinium, a goose feather is inserted into the nose, after being wrapped with a soft cloth; and it happens that it holds the nasal fracture more firmly, and does not prevent the patient from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that: and if you wish, use licinia.
And if it happens that the nose is affected again while you are treating a warm abscess, apply plasters to the nose with cerote, or with suet infused in rose oil, or a little of diachylon.
But if a warm abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to apply plasters made with sesame flour and incense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft cloth over it, and do not bind the nose at all.
But if the bones of the nose are slightly fractured or crushed, then it is necessary to incise over them and extract them with suitable instruments. Then sew the incision and care for it with what promotes fleshing and strengthening, using appropriate plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose, then you must treat it with lint: and administer a lead tube until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then examine if the fracture is only external and not broken into two parts, and bent inward: then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient: similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the bulge of the fracture from inside to the outside easily with your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and align it accordingly.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you must apply traction from both sides, according to straightness: until it is possible to align it.
If, however, the teeth have already been displaced or broken: then bind those that you hope will remain with gold thread, or silver thread, or silk. Then place wax over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a properly made large splint on top of the cloth: or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it according to how its bandage is prepared, and apply it appropriately until it is not destroyed: and instruct the patient to maintain tranquility and rest: and provide him a diet of mild liquid foods.
If however you think that something has already altered in its form in some way: then hurry and untie it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and make a poultice of mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the tumors: and place soft wool on the poultice.
Therefore, while that poultice remains on it and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it alone, do not untie it until it heals: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound in three weeks.
If during this time a warm abscess occurs, then apply what we have said many times for its reduction, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, take note. For if a fragment is already separated from the bone or from the soft tissue: then be subtle in this, to remove those fragments from the soft tissue with whatever suits you among the instruments for their removal.
If however the wound opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those soft tissues and nothing of them remains, sew the wound opening if it is wide: and if not, apply a poultice suitable for it, and promote fleshing over it until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of the clavicle when it is fractured.
The collarbone is mostly fractured from the front part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two parts, besides which contusions may occur: and it is easier to restore it.
Or it happens that contusions occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or it occurs that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the procedure when the fracture is without a wound is to have two assistants present, so that one of them holds an aid following the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form becomes as it should be: and there should not be any prominence or concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit. Its size should be according to your need: extend and lift the collarbone, and compress the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has gone deep, then the patient must lie down on his neck. Place a moderately large pillow under his shoulder, and have an assistant press his shoulder downward until the end of the collarbone that is deep inside is raised upward. Then correct and equalize the fracture with your fingers.
If however you feel a splinter from the collarbone and it moves: then it is necessary to cut above it and remove the splinter with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone, then skill is needed in cutting it with one of the files you have prepared for this, after you place under the collarbone the instrument that keeps sifach: and it is an instrument made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that does not have a concavity.
And its width should be according to what you need from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length should also be according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the disruption of the wound which you made when you extracted the splinter of the bone is wide, and it is threatened by a warm abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a warm abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the amount of the wound's tear.
If a warm abscess does occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of it.
A narrowing of the bone which is without a wound and without a cut, is treated by placing on the bone a poultice made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft plug on it. Then place the ball under his scrotum, if you need that. Then take the himematam, that is, the roll which the Saracens use, which should be very long: and its width should be one palm, or around that. Then place a doubled poultice on the plug and ointment. Then take a strip from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the strip in place of the himematam which falls over the fracture and area. Then place the himematam over the fracture as described, and wrap it around his neck, and under his healthy scrotum, and under his injured scrotum: and bring it back over all parts, and in such a way that you see the binding holds the fracture tightly: and this is not hidden from you, and all of it so that the strip is not removed from the mouth of the fracture. And for this reason, it is necessary to look after the patient every day. Whenever the bandage softens and you see that the strip can now be removed: then set it right, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back, and put a small pillow under his scrotum at his side during the night, so that his support is lifted from his side with it. The fracture of the fork should be raised by elevating the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the bandage if no accident occurs from itching or from an abscess until the twelfth day.
Then renew the plaster if you see that it is needed. And add to the binding, and leave it until it is restored and coagulated.
And the fracture of the fork is bound and strengthened as much as possible within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it occurs in some men in a shorter time.
Regarding the restoration of the fractured spatula.
The spatula is rarely fractured, and if it is, it happens at its wide part: and from it, only its edges are fractured.
When therefore that part is fractured, and it is fractured in its middle, and it is not recognized except by touch: then according to the figure of the fracture attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural shape by any means of ingenuity that is possible for you.
Then place flour dust with egg white on the area, and a soft tow, and put a cloth poultice over it. Then place on it a wide strip from a thin board according to the size of the entire spatula, or slightly larger than it.
If however there is a concavity in some part of the spatula's place, then equalize that concavity with soft tow until the probe descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with appropriate constriction, and the firmness of the probe, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage is softened, tighten it, and equalize the probe if it is moved from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When this number is complete, then loosen the bandage: and you will be in safety. For it is of bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
If a fragment proceeds from the bone and pricks under the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do what we have said in the case of a clavicle fracture from the sedation of a hot abscess, if anything should happen from it.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured rib.
The chest is sometimes fractured in the middle of it, and this rarely occurs. Its extremities are often fractured and shattered from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, which inclines toward the lower parts, and violent pain befalls him, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is apparent in the broken bone, and it is not hidden from the senses: Its restoration is for the sick person to lie on his back: and place under his Spatulas a pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and bring the ribs together from both sides or sides with your hands: and adjust in its alignment, in every way that is possible and appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place on it a poultice and tow, and put above a rod from a thin board of willow, or chalinus, and those similar in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth. Then adjust in binding it over the fractured bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage around to the back several times, and secure it with a proper tightness.
Then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if the necessity of the case calls for its removal due to itching, or corrosion which occurs in the area, or pain, or swelling: then hurry and remove it, and take off the poultice, and treat what happens with the method of its cure.
Then apply the poultice again if you see that it is necessary: and secure it with a bandage until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts following the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And attrition does not occur to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden to the sense during the examination with fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the fracture is evenly aligned with fingers according to the possible manner, until the shape is even as it should be. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured ends with a splint, if it requires that.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the interior: then the patient suffers severe pain and a puncture, like the puncture that occurs to one who has pleurisy: therefore, because the bone pierces the membrane, and also the patient experiences difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many methods for this: some of them say that you should give the patient foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the stomach becomes inflamed, expands, and pushes the fracture to the exterior.
We, however, refrain from causing a quick reason to produce a warm abscess, if it does not occur. If, however, it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: place a suction device on the area, then suck with strength, and it is similar in reasoning. However, it is feared that the suction may attract excesses to the area due to the condition of weakness.
And some of them said: the area must be covered with wool which has already been immersed in warm oil: and poultices should be placed between the ribs until it is filled: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then the patient should be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If a violent thing constrains the patient, which the patient cannot tolerate, and the wound pierces the covering with a damaging puncture, and we are worried about the patient: then it is necessary to cut open above the area and expose the fractured rib. Then we place upon it the instrument that preserves the seam, of which the description has preceded: and cut the bone with ease, and cut into it.
Then bring together the two edges of the wound if it is large with sutures: and treat it with plasters until it heals.
If a warm abscess should occur in the meantime, then hurry and pour poultices into rose oil, and place them on the area: and treat the patient with what is appropriate for an abscess.
In the meantime, he should lie on his side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Regarding the treatment of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
The bones of the neck sometimes suffer fractures, and rarely do they experience attrition: and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when it happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then have confidence. For if you see his hands already softened, numb, and lifeless: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them: and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in many conditions it is mortal.
But if he moves them and feels the contraction with fingers and the pricking: then know that the nuchal bone is sound, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If a similar thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and what happened to the hands happens to them: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excrement are discharged involuntarily: and when he is placed on his stomach, excrement is discharged involuntarily: and when he is placed on his back and wants to urinate but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If however none of that happens to him, the case is milder.
And the treatment of what happens from that is to try to reduce the heat of the abscess: thus, you place on the spine ground rose oil alone: or with eggs of a roasted egg.
Place that on it several times a day until the hot abscess is reduced; then apply one of the strengthening, drying, and binding plasters to the area and bind it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility. Nor should he sleep on the side where he finds pain until it is healed.
If it happens near the grinding of the sathaiated bone, or something that is already separated from it, then it is necessary to incise over the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture if it is large. Then treat it with some flesh-forming ointment until it is healed.
If the last bone of the alhausos is fractured, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and by a second adjustment.
Then place something on it and remove it: and treat the wound with the preceding treatment until it is healed.
Regarding the restoration of the anchor.
Chapter Eleven.
The bones of the anchor rarely fracture. If they are broken, then their fracture is not complete, but rather a fragmentation at the ends of them: and they split lengthwise, and lean inward: and the sick person experiences pain in the place and a pricking sensation: and the leg that is broken becomes numb.
And the restoration of it is to extend your hand over it until you stand over the fracture and understand its configuration.
If the fracture is only at the ends of it, then equalize that fracture according to how it is prepared for you by equalization until it resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or has already leaned inward: then make the sick person lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared. When therefore you have equalized it, place a poultice on it: then place a splint of wood or leather on it: and bind with a bandage on which you do not fear the displacement of the fracture nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of them with what fills it until the bandage holds it according to equality: and instruct the sick person to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
If a hot abscess occurs to him: then cease from extending it and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides: and place on it what causes it to subside, as previously mentioned, then return to its restoration: and bind it as appropriate.
If there is pus in the bone or if it is fragmented, remove it.
On the restoration of the fractured Adiutorium.
Adiutorium is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Spatula.
If it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: to take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form that follows.
And two bands shall be tied at the two ends.
Then it shall be suspended from an elevated place: and the patient shall sit on a seat. Then he shall throw his fractured arm over the wood until it reaches the armpit, where the curvature of the wood is attached in the middle.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that it extends the limb downward. Then the physician equalizes the fracture with his hands simultaneously, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is to have the patient lie on their back: and suspend their arm from their neck with a band. Then command two assistants, so that one of them holds what is above the fracture with his hands: and the other holds below it, and each extends to their respective part.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with one band, and above it with another band: and each of the two assistants shall extend them to their respective parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a bandage be placed on that place and on the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort until when the fracture is aligned as it should be and applied properly: then bind it, if a warm abscess does not occur.
If a warm abscess occurs, then release the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess quiets: then bind it: and the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a folding of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be bound with support, and let his open hand be placed on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm with support, so that the arm remains in its proper place: if nothing prohibits you from it, nor anything alters from the fractured bone.
If you fear that anything from the fractured bone may alter, then use splints: and it is done by placing a splint over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place the width of a finger between each splint: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an additional three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints the binding which I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your binding over the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the more the fracture is elongated, let the binding be looser.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the binding as we said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do this: and if you fear a warm abscess, then remove the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then examine the binding for all three days, so that there does not occur in the place itching, or swelling, or prevention of food reaching the limb, due to the arrangement of the excess of the binding. Therefore, correct all of that according to what we said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the binding unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hand on his stomach: and place under his support a cushion full of wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the fractured limb is not loosened, or the binding softened. Therefore, correct all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have mentioned, so that the food is light at first, until the swelling begins to decrease: then it is necessary to make his food more substantial.
For the custom of aiding and the leg is to be bound for forty days: then it must be loosened, and a bath administered, and treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is ugly and crushed: then do not loosen the bandage and splint from it until fifty days, and even up to two months.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones.
Of which one is smaller: and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is larger, and it is the one that is placed below the smaller one on the lower part.
Perhaps only the larger forearm bone is fractured, or the smaller one: and perhaps both are fractured at the same time.
When the smaller forearm bone is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm bone is fractured, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are fractured at the same time.
If the bone that is fractured is the upper smaller forearm bone, then it is appropriate for the physician in restoring it to apply slight extension with ease until it is aligned.
If the larger forearm bone is the one that is fractured: then it must be strongly tightened greatly.
And it is necessary that he places the form of the hand at its restoration, and its extension spread out on the pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers: and let the lower joint be common to all the fingers, or the rest: and the patient sitting square upon himself, and let the pillow be before him, or in line with him, in height, so that the patient does not incur labor.
Then let the minister extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a bandage. And another minister shall extend similarly from above.
Then the doctor equalizes the bone until he restores it to the best shape possible.
If there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then strive to replace every fragment in its place by your skill.
If there appears a separated splinter and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of its restoration: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method we have mentioned previously.
If the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for that, so take the treatment from there.
If a warm abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line cloth with cerote made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be between thick and thin: and bind the cloth tightly over it with a fine bandage until the abscess is calmed: then remove the cerote, and apply a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place the splints, and let the splint which is placed upon the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And you shall know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture be in one shaft, or in two shafts at once.
Then place your binding over the site of the fracture stronger and more forceful, and somewhat more with the binding toward the upper part of the fracture, or towards the lower part of it: or place a milder binding similar to its binding, according to which was recalled at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture be soft, moist cloths: and they should not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of flax, properly between fine and coarse, as we have mentioned.
And consider the limb and the bandage after some days. And if something happens that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to foment the limb with warm water until the itching ceases, and leave the limb unconstricted until it rests, then bind it again.
And if the binding has already been softened, and the bone has already been set, and similar things: then correct all of that with your skill, and examine it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, then it is necessary to soften it somewhat and leave it for several days until the nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
If however none of those things which we mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be dissolved, unless after twenty days, or around this number.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended to his neck. And let his neck be kept still, and protected by its strength from disturbing movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that depends on the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the knuckles of the hand and the joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to him, except through frequent friction.
When therefore a fracture or attrition happens to the sole, then it is necessary that the patient sit upright, and before him there be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the fractured bones, and the doctor should align them until they are joined well together.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if a warm abscess does not occur. Then place the splint over it according to the size of the area: and you have now wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is around the lower part near the belly of the foot, then make something like a sphere out of cloth: and instruct the patient to press it with their fractured foot over it: then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be made of leather, which should have softness, so that it can be applied with the skin to the body of the foot: and bind it according to what is necessary.
If the fracture is on the outer side, then it is necessary to place a splint on top and another splint below on the foot: and let the hand be held straight. Then place the binding as the hand is rotated: and extend it between the fingers with a bandage.
If a fracture occurs in one of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, adjust it according to what is necessary. Then bind it with the sole: and if you wish, place a straight, small splint on it: so that the fracture is raised and does not move.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, and the index finger, or the little finger, and the ring finger, then align it and bind it with the healthy finger following it: or bind them all according to continuity: and this is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of warm poultice: and apply what is necessary when something happens to it, according to how its narration is repeated.
On the restoration of the fractured hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is noticeable because it turns to the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a band is tightened above the fracture, and another band below the fracture. And the patient should lie down on his face.
Then the attendant should stretch all the band to its part according to equality: and this is done when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip bone, then the band should be placed at the part of the pecten so that the extension falls upward: and another band below the fracture.
And so similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then the band should be placed near the knee, so that the extension is downward.
Then the doctor equalizes the bone with both of his hands until he restores it according to the similarity of its natural form: and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and binding, if the member does not develop an abscess. If an abscess develops in it, then leave it for a few days until the warm abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
The binding of it should be done thus. You must wrap a hard cloth, that is, a bandage, over the fracture; and fold it twice or three times; and let there be an excess remaining from it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both sides. Then wrap over the leg and thigh what remains from the extra cloth.
Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and put one of them over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the binding is even.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wraps. And the further you extend with the bandage from the site of the fracture, let your binding be looser and softer, and gentler.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg. Bind therefore with both three splints which are on top. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part until you reach the ankle. And with both, tighten also the ends of the splints from the other side so that the binding does not move from its place.
Then release the tourniquet from that area while it remains so that there does not occur in the member corrosion, nor abscess, nor swelling, and similar to these. If anything happens from that. Hurry therefore with its solution, and correct everything that happens from it, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a fragment of bone in the bone that pains: then it is necessary to equally remove it, if it is possible for you. And if not, find it and remove it: and treat it as previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, to which splints are added, as we have said in the case of support and arm.
However, in the restoration of such a fracture, the patient does not become lame. And if it is restored alone, without the other leg being applied to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs limps always.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little is added or subtracted: all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
Regarding the restoration of the fractured kneecap.
Know that fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but wear often happens to it.
If however a fracture occurs to it, then not unless either a裂隙或孔在其部位。 And it is with the wound, and without the wound. And it stands over the whole thing with sensation.
And the restoration is such that the disrupted parts are equalized with fingers until they come together and join as much as possible by equalization and ease, and construction.
Then apply a plaster made from sesame flour or mill flour with egg white: and place a round splint on it if you need it: and bind it securely with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the treatments we have described for other fractures: such as warm poultices and similar things: so that you oppose every occurrence with what is appropriate to it until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the legs consist of two bones, one of which is thick and is named the leg bone: and the other is slender, and is named the fibula.
And it happens to both types of fractures as it happens to the two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is like the restoration of the arm equally, and the procedure is the same.
If both bones are fractured simultaneously, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the slender bone is fractured, the leg turns to the front part.
And if a thicker bone is fractured, you find it below: and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalization, and ligation of the splints according to that equally.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a nasty fracture, having many sethagiae: that there be less extension, and milder alleviation by you.
And in the leg indeed there is something of an operation similar to the arm: and it is that when you equalize the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which is used in the manufacture of coverings which is placed under boards: and they are made from palm ribs: and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and neither should they be from the thin ones: and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee downward.
Then wrap cloth over each one with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee downward to the foot.
Then bind the two bands at three places from both ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg bends to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now a cradle made of wood according to the length of the leg should be administered, and the leg should be placed in it, so that it keeps it from movement.
And it is indeed very important that you do this in a fracture when it is accompanied by a proper wound.
Then consider the leg every day, and be diligent with the same last degree of care to prevent abscess, or swelling, or other such things. If therefore any of these things happens, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A complete fracture does not occur in the calcaneus.
Fractures do sometimes occur in the bones of the foot and occasionally in the toes as well; and in most cases, they only suffer abrasions.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones rise above their natural position, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that which has risen from those bones; then press or tread on it until it is leveled and returns to its proper place.
Then apply a plaster and place some tow on top of it: and put a small board under the arch of the foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it to the foot with a suitable bandage after you wrap it with cloths and other necessary materials.
Therefore, when three days pass by him, or four, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones equal, whether they are fractured or dislocated. The operation on them is as you see.
If some fingers are fractured, then restore them and align them as I have described to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it: then place under the foot the board I have described to you, and secure it with an appropriate bandage.
If however what is fractured of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on every finger a splint of its size, wrapped in a linen cloth: and secure the board at the bottom of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds firmly.
And it is fitting for you, so that you do not forget, to consider all that I have told you regarding other fractures from the accidents we mentioned: and address each accident with what is appropriate.
Regarding the fracture of the female vulva, and the os pubis, and the male penis.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then tilt her to the side of her back slightly, and have her held from behind.
Then the midwife should fill her vulva with fat until it is full: and let it become spherical in the vulva from it.
Then the woman should be shaken, and her back raised gradually. For that fat comes out, until it becomes spherical in front of the vulva's entrance, and the broken part returns.
Then place a poultice on her back. When she wants to urinate, she should remove the fat easily, then urinate: and insert it back in the manner you initially introduced it; then return to her original poultice.
She should do this for seven days, or so, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, and attach a reed tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns to its place.
Then it should be filled with fat and remain for several days, as we have mentioned, until it heals.
And when the opening of the Pectinus of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and realignment what we have mentioned for the Anchae bone.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures that slightly affect it, especially in one who has some practice or use.
And understand my book very much, for the fracture itself shows you the correct way in many arrangements for its restoration and constriction: therefore understand.
In the case of a man's penis when it is fractured, take the throat of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place a wrapping of cloth over it, and let it be wrapped, and leave it for three days, or around that time, and it will be healed, if God wills.
On the restoration of fractured bones when there is a wound.
It is appropriate for one to whom a fracture with a wound has occurred, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the scapula, or similar, that you hurry and bleed him immediately: if the conditions for bleeding are indicated to you, as we have premised.
If the wound is bleeding, then you must hurry to treat it by dusting ground zegi over it: if you have nothing else available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, unless an abscess occurs.
If an abscess does occur that is warm, then postpone the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause harmful complications to occur.
If however a fractured bone protrudes above the skin: then it is necessary that you attempt to reduce it and equalize it with your hands using ease and slight traction.
If however the traction and its equalization with your hands are not prepared: then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument that is made of iron, whose length is seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for this reason, it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of them according to the amount needed for that cure in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let it have a small thickness, so that it does not double under pressure on it during the operation: and let it have a sharp end, which has an edge at its ends. And let the upper part of it be thickened from the middle to the lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation to place its sharp curved end over the protruding bone end: and push it with it once, until the bone returns and is equalized by some equalization.
Therefore, attempt to equalize the ends of the fracture closely.
If the ends of it are fractured finely and the instrument cannot grasp it properly, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
If indeed you cannot reduce them with what we have described entirely, then cut it open with whatever is suitable for it from the incisors which we mentioned: or saw it open with one of the saws in whichever way possible for you.
Then scrape what remains on the bone from roughness and small bark.
When the bone is reduced, and the patient finds severe painful damage after its reduction, then you know that the bone did not return to its natural position.
Do therefore that. For you provide great assistance to the patient when the restoration you perform on the bone is completed.
Then soak the cloth in astringent, black wine. And especially if it is in summer. And you shall not place wax on the wound, nor anything that contains oil: so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then apply the splints at the completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed: so that you cut with scissors a hole in the coverings according to the size of the wound.
And be cautious with all caution, lest you compress the wound with the fracture. For foolish doctors have often done this: and have caused death, or corruption, or pus, that is, sanies, to occur in their patients. And let your bandage be soft, gentle, different from the bandages of other fractures.
If the wound is severe, or it is a large wound, and you fear some bad occurrences related to it as we have mentioned, and you find the pain suspended in that place: then it is not appropriate to apply boards on top of it. And make involutions from hard cloths instead of boards, and bind it with them.
When it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove the cloth you placed over it with wine. Then apply licina, and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as the tetrapharmacum ointment, and similar ones.
And it is appropriate for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every morning and evening until it is stabilized and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus may flow downward easily.
If many days pass for the wound and it does not stabilize, nor does the pus separate from it: then you know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. How many of those splinters are separated, remove them, and extract them. And those among them that are not separated, and they prick the member, and cause pain to occur, endeavor to cut them, and remove them in every way that is possible for you.
If it happens that the wound suffers from gangrene, or ecchymosis, that is, discoloration, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to oppose each one of them with what is suitable for it in treatment, of which a preceding account has been given in its chapter.
And from that in which my speech must stand, and to which you should give your present mind when a bone is fractured and protrudes over the member, such as the hip bone, and helper bones, and similar ones from large members, it is that you should not presume its attraction, for death often results from that; rather leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wound if it seems right to you: and if not, leave it.
Regarding the treatment of nodulation in the area of a fracture.
This nodulation often occurs at the site of a fracture healing. Especially those near the joints: and the figure of the member is disfigured by it. And it may prevent the member from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the nodulation is recent: apply to it medications that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar substances.
Thus, you take some of these or all of them: and finish them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the knot in tow, and bind them well over it.
And leave the binding, nor loosen it for many days. Then remove it, and repeat another, until the knot is removed.
Or bind it over the sheet of lead appropriately. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the members.
If however the knot has already turned to stone and is bound tightly, and necessity urgently requires its removal: then find on it from its upper part, and cut off the prominent excess: or scrape it with some scrapers until it is removed: and care for it until it heals.
Regarding the treatment when it is restored, and the member remains delicate afterwards, not according to its natural state.
When the fracture of the bone is restored, and the member remains delicate and weak afterwards: then this happens only due to many causes. One of which is the frequency of loosening and binding the bandage not as it should be: or due to the excess tightness of the bindings, preventing nourishment from flowing to the member: or due to the frequent application of excessive embrocation at inappropriate times: or due to the lack of blood in the body of the patient and their weakness.
And the treatment is to feed the sick person and make his body weak until blood multiplies in him. And administer a bath. And bring joy and happiness to him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch may attract much nourishment to it. And repeatedly rub it with warm water until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal crookedly.
