When it happens that after the restoration and healing of a limb, there is crookedness, prominence of the fractured bone, or knotting, and the form of the member is marred by this, yet the limb is not hindered in its natural function, then you should not accept the advice of him who thinks the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly disgraceful, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to rub it with warm water in which softening herbs have been decocted, such as the leaves of marshmallow and its root, and sweet clover, and things like these. And a poultice should be made for it with a softening plaster, such as properly made diachylon.
Either take the mucilage of the root of althea, and mix it with chicken fat, or sesamin oil: and plaster with it.
Either take fat figs, and pound them with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among the medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes knotting is solved with frequent, old, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And the movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
If however the tortuousness has already preceded, and become strong, and turned into stone, and necessitated the need for treatment with iron: then it is necessary to cut the upper part of it, and release the continuity of the bone, and cut away what is excessive from the knot or bone with fine scalpel: and facilitate movement in it with care and attention.
Regarding the discussion on dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of some joint from its place. Therefore, it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the member is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and injuries to the patient.
When therefore dislocation happens to someone, then one must hurry to restore it: do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the area may become inflamed, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, it should not be moved or extended during the hour of its suppuration. For it often causes spasms and painful pains.
But when it happens, then you must quickly proceed to bloodletting of the patient. Then it should be left until the suppuration rests.
Then the limb should be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And let the entire limb be treated with it, whose memory will come at its proper time.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as it preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
Regarding the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated to a slight degree.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places slightly, and are softened for a moment. Or they are dislocated completely and entirely until they soften towards the chest, until the saliva of the patient runs and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When however its displacement is small, then it returns in most cases by itself with little effort.
If however the displacement is complete and entire: then it must be that the reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed entirely: and it is that the operator hold the head of the patient: and the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the displaced jaw between its mouth, if the displacement is on one side. Or let him insert two thumbs simultaneously, if the displacement is on both sides. And the other fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he equalizes.
And command the patient to clean his jaws and prepare them for eating on all sides. And the physician equalizes the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously: then apply vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the patient is closed, and do not loosen: then place upon them small pieces of cloth with plaster that is made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be bound easily with soft bands.
And let the patient sleep on his back: and let his head stand between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left: nor should he attempt, or be forced to chew anything: rather, let his food be given by a gentle sip, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is set: then let him eat what he wants, and let it be administered easily.
Let not one labor over the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is set, and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then often fevers and persistent swelling result from this, and perhaps the stomach of the patient liquefies, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When therefore you see that: then know that it is a loss, and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Regarding the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the shoulder.
The collarbone does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outward, and this is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is for the patient to lie on their back and extend their arms. Then force the area with your palm strongly, for it will return to its place.
Then apply a poultice and bandages over it, and bind it tightly.
The extremities that follow the elbow and are connected to it do not dislocate, except rarely. If it dislocates at some time, then it is necessary to reduce and align it as we have said, and prepare it accordingly.
Then place over it a poultice, and powder, and a bandage. And command the sick person to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment the shoulder extremity is reduced when it is also displaced from its position.
On the reduction of dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the outer lower part. The second is that it is dislocated towards the chest. And it may also be dislocated towards the upper part of the humerus: and this rarely occurs.
And it does not dislocate towards the back, because of the spur. And it does not dislocate towards the front, because of the nerves.
And it is dislocated and protrudes most towards the lower outer part, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. For it protrudes quickly in them and recedes quickly.
In those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely that it protrudes with difficulty and recedes with difficulty.
And it may happen to some people that they suffer a blow or fall, and the humerus swells with a hot swelling, and it is assumed because of this that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it until you stand on its certainty, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is directed downwards towards the armpit: and you distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy shoulder. For you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and beneath the armpit when touching the head of the humerus it feels almost like an egg: and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is to have the attendant raise the hand upwards. Then place your thumbs beneath its armpit, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the attendant should raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then place it below, for it returns quickly.
If it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation first occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and a massage that softens and soothes should be administered, such as if the roots of coltsfoot, fenugreek, and sweet clover are boiled in water, and applied.
Then the patient should lie on his back: and place under his buttocks a sphere and wool, which should be equal in softness and firmness.
Then the doctor should place his heel on the sphere, and raise the shoulder joint with force, and pull the patient's hand towards the lower part.
And if you wish, return him according to this method: it is that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the buttocks of the patient: and raise his buttocks upward until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand towards the lower part of his abdomen. If the patient is light: then it is necessary to suspend something else with him to weight him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and that is to fix a long piece of wood in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like the sleeve of a mortar: which should not be thick nor thin. Then place under the patient, after soft cloths are placed on top of the head of the wood, and let the patient stand along the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand downward from one side, and stretch his body with force from the other side: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If the reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood whose length is equal to two arms, and its width equal to four fingers, and its thickness equal to two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entry into the wound is eased according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be bound over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the wounded area.
Then it should be placed under the wounded arm of the patient, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood down to the lower part: and the wood should be bound over the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder across its width, and the hand should be extended downward, and the rest of the body should be left hanging from the other side. For the joint moves immediately.
When therefore its insertion is complete in whatever way possible: then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the wound. Then apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with frankincense, and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is spread on top.
Then bind under the wound with a suitable bandage: and wrap the binding over the plaster on top: and attach his hand to his neck, and leave it, so that his hand is not moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be limited until the limb is strengthened: because it is faster for its healing.
Then it should be reopened after seven days, or five, and exercised with movement. If therefore it is firm and does not soften, then it is already healed.
If however the joint is dislocated frequently, due to moisture that affects it, or for another reason: then it is necessary to apply a cautery with three holes to it, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cauteries.
If all this is done, and the ligament loosens after seven days, and the joint does not become firm, and the poultice and binding are reapplied several times, and it does not become firm, and falls down, and cannot be raised upwards: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus have been cut, or stretched, or softened: and you know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of a dislocation that occurs towards the chest and nipple, or towards the back, is performed with impulse and extension by hand: until it returns, and the remaining binding and care are applied to it, until it heals.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use a bath frequently until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original state.
Regarding the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and it also returns with difficulty: and it can be dislocated in all directions, but especially towards the front or the back.
And its displacement is not hidden from you: for it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever shape it is displaced. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly shown to you. For the joint is concaved, and it cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its shoulder with it.
And you must hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot swelling occurs.
For if a hot swelling occurs, the reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not heal altogether, especially when it is toward the back. For it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, and of more severe pain: and death often results from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is one of those that can be reduced, is to extend his hand with two hands: and his arm should be expanded, and the physician's hands should be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with his thumbs simultaneously, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is to the front: then it is reduced by doubling his hand with his own hand until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a strong, very powerful extension: and it is done by two assistants extending the arm, and two assistants holding it so that it does not move during the extension.
Then the arm is rotated to all sides after a doubled long cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped over his hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with oil: so that it is one of those things which help in lubricating the joint with ease. Then push the joint with a strong force until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place on it a poultice that has astringency and drying with egg white: and bind it with a suitable tight bandage. And suspend the arm from the patient's neck: and leave it for several days.
Then it should be loosened. If then the joint is fixed in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then apply the poultice again, and the bandage, and leave it for several days until it is bound. Then loosen it.
If however heaviness befalls him after the reduction of the joint, and the position is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it straightens, or place on the joint fat Euphorbia peplus. Then it should be bandaged, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and insert it into the bath. When therefore he sweats, then friction the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat on it once, and second, and third with the entry of the bath, until it is relieved.
And if you wish, place on it cow dung, wet, heated with butter, and bind over it.
Do this several times. For it is indeed relieved and returns to its normal state.
On the treatment of dislocation by Almahassem.
Mahassem's hand is dislocated frequently, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, for the other joints. However, it is necessary that the speed be in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a warm abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation is to place Mahassem's affected hand on a board, and have an assistant extend his hand; and the doctor should place his palm over the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns.
However, it must be considered if the palm has already retreated to the belly of the hand: then the patient should place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inward on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls over the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a poultice soothing the swelling: and leave it, do not repeat that.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you do anything about its reduction, after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its curvature: and it does not harm the patient anything, except that the hand is weakened, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are cut or worn.
There is therefore no other method except to bind it with a cautery near the site of the injury. And perhaps it does not help it at all.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then apply to it the poultice that we have mentioned. Then it should be bound and left for five days: afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
If however its movement is impaired, and something serious occurs in it: then soften it with warm water and rubbing several times until it is softened.
Regarding the treatment of the displacement of fingers.
When one of the fingers is displaced in any direction, whether towards the back of the palm or its front: then extend the finger and push the displacement with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the side to which it is displaced, and leave it for two days.
Then hang him and stretch him until he is straight on his day.
When therefore it will be night, bind him according to this account: and do not cease to loosen him during the day: and exercise him with movement: and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until it is stretched.
And do likewise with it if the hand is dislocated towards the abdomen: and bind it to that part, and perform your first treatment on it until it heals.
And do likewise with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Regarding the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae.
When it happens that someone has a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places: then there is no treatment for it, because death approaches the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the excretion of the sick person exits without will, and they cannot hold it: and often some of their limbs are weakened, or both of their legs, or both of their arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is displaced from its place.
For it often happens that they are displaced to all four parts.
That which is displaced backward is called Gibbositas.
The treatment, however, is to consider then, if the Gibbositas occurred from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor any healing at all.
In those which occur from a fall, or blows, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken at length about the methods of treatment. Of which much does not return with profit.
But I have already abbreviated from that, of which little is excused from much due to its proximity to my intention: and in my explanation whereby I explained it, and formed its instrument in a different way from that in which they showed and explained it.
I say therefore, that in the humpback condition which occurs on the front side, there is neither remedy nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not cured from them, except that which occurs on the back properly, with what I narrate: and it is that the infirm should be extended on his face on an equal podium near the wall: and a wet straw should be spread under him so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight piece of wood should be placed and fixed in a socket in the ground at the end of the podium near his head: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the podium: and the assistant should hold the wood, and it should not be firmly fixed in the socket. And another assistant should hold the other one.
Then wrap over the head of the infirm and under his armpits a soft, strong band: and extend the end of the band to the wood that is by his head: and tie it there.
Then tie another band above his thighs, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bands together, and tie them to another stick which is at his feet.
Then let all the ministers stretch the band to the end of the stick, and let not the two sticks be moved from the places in which they are fixed: however, they should not be fastened as we said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the spine with force until it returns to its proper position; or he may place a board on it. Then attach his feet to the board until it returns.
If it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And make in the wall that we mentioned is near the patient, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And let the doctor put his feet on the other end. And let him pull firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a device that is wrapped around with your hand: and it is such that you fix into the ground near the head of the patient at the back of the foot two sticks, whose length is one arm: and near his head at the back of the foot two sticks.
And let the length which is between all the wood be one palm: and now make a hole in every place where the turn runs: and secure all the wood very much on the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert the round wood: and it is a turn where the ligament is revolved in two holes of two woods.
And at its end is a hole, in which the wood is secured, whose length is one palm with that which revolves: and in two other pieces of wood is similarly that.
Then tighten the ligaments which you tightened on the chest of the patient to the turn which is at his head: and the ligaments which you tightened on his legs to the turn which is at his feet.
Then let the assistant stand by every turn, who turns his hand with wax with which the turn revolves: and the physician aligns the spine according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turn that follows.
Then after the spine returns and the place is aligned, then it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then apply tow. Then place on top of the plaster a strip of board whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length should be the amount that covers the hump and the healthy part of the spine.
Then it should be bound with the bandage we mentioned. And let the sick person use a subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if something of the prominence remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and alleviate be administered, along with the application of the plaster we mentioned for a long time. And now it is applied with that sheet of lead.
And sometimes there is a prominence in the last vertebra of the spine: and it is thought that by it a dislocation occurs, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, it should not be presumed to use this medicine on him: for it may cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and the shoulder only suffer dislocation: they do not experience what happens to other joints due to slight displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the ankle dislocates in four directions. It is because it dislocates towards the front and towards the back.
And it dislocates most towards the inside: and only slightly towards the front and the back.
And the treatment for its dislocation towards the inside is as follows: when you align the healthy leg of the patient with the injured one, it appears longer: and his knee protrudes more than that of a healthy person, and the patient cannot double his foot at the groin: and the area following the groin becomes inflamed with a warm swelling, because the head of the femur has now reached there.
And the sign of him to whom happens dislocation outward is that his accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation forward happens is that he extends the sound leg according to complement: however, he does not double it without pain which is in the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot move forward over it: and his urine is retained, and his groin swells, and during walking there is a dragging of his heel.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation backward happens is that he does not extend the knee, nor can he double it before he doubles the groin: and his leg is even shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip bone is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this species of dislocation is to consider then, if the dislocation is old, which already has aged with the person having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its own disposition: then there is no cure in it at all. Therefore, it should not be completely presumed for him.
In the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and it is from one of the four species of dislocation: then hurry to this, to turn the joint, and extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left: for perhaps it will return, and will not require further treatment.
If it does not return this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant, and have him extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a bandage, which should be tied above his leg over the knee: and another assistant extends his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under the tendons. Then bind with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the bandage with the other hand.
And let the extension be either from the front part of the groin to the shoulder part, or from the back part to the dorsal part.
And let the extension of all be at once, until the sick person is lifted with their body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every type to do what I will say about the proper cure.
In its proper reduction when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side. Then place the bandage over the root of the thigh, in the area between the head of the thigh and the region below the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to a higher part of the body, toward the shoulder area.
Then let another strong minister take his arms, and compress the thick part of the injured thigh, and extend outward with a forceful stretch. For it now returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced.
If it slips out of your control and does not obey you for entry with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to bind both feet of the sick person simultaneously with a strong yet gentle bandage over the two heels and over the two knees: and let the length of each be four fingers from its counterpart: and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the length of two fingers.
Then let the patient be suspended over a beam in the house: and its length from the ground should be two arms' lengths.
Then command a strong youth to secure the head of the thigh: and he should be suspended with the patient being held by another; and another youth should push, securing the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In returning it to its proper place, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the patient lie on a platform, as we have described in the case of one having a hump. And a band should be tied above his diseased leg and above his chest.
Then two sticks should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then a additional stick should be placed in the middle of the platform, fixed well: on which wrapped wet cloths should be placed, so that it does not harm the patient: and there should be a stick between his thighs, so that it is not pulled downward during the extension.
Then let all the assistants pull at their end, and the doctor equalize the reduction with his hand.
If it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the same board and press it as we have said in the case of humpback, equally. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the limb of the patient be extended quickly: and according to this same account on a platform: and the physician should place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the patient. Then he should compress it with the other hand, and with that compression place it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and he should be raised above the ground: rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, as is necessary when his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips: and it is on his face, and the ligaments stretched: according to what we have recently said.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board over the place to which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the patient: and when you see them equal, and the patient pulls his leg and extends it without hesitation: then you know that the limb has returned as it should be: then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind with imemati, that is, a wheel: so that the hip does not move in any direction: and the patient should adhere to rest for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then you know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore release the patient to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then undo it and make him walk slowly on both for several days until he is well strengthened.
Regarding the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If it does not adhere to the hip: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is to make the patient sit straight, so that he extends his leg if he can over it, and your back adheres to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and place them straight over his knee between your fingers. Then apply the two sides of his knee with force using your palms. And another assistant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction is that the leg adheres to the hip with ease, not laboriously.
Then apply plasters to it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with wool for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And the patient should not use walking for several days until healed.
If the reduction of it resists you, then administer strong extension with ligaments, the use of which was previously described in the treatment of anchor, until it returns.
Regarding the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes displaced slightly, and sometimes dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment for its displacement is easy reduction: it is to extend it with ease by hand and equalize it until it returns.
In treating it when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary to make the patient sit straight: and let a strong attendant hold him from the back part of his spine, at its midpoint.
Then hold with your right hand the foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the heel.
Then draw the foot towards yourself with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the ball of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have said.
Then push the ball of the foot towards the leg on the third attempt, and you pull out the thorn.
If it returns in one attempt, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot even, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it: because it will return.
If the reduction is prevented despite what we have mentioned: then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and fix a stake in the ground very firmly, so that it falls between his thighs: and now you have wrapped cloth around it, so that it does not harm the patient.
Then the assistant should take hold of his thigh: afterwards the assistant should extend the other foot, either with his hands, or with a band that ties above the ankle. Then the assistant should stretch fully against the extension of his counterpart: and the stake should stand between the two thighs of the patient holding him so that his body is not drawn downward during the extension.
Then the doctor should equalize the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant should hold the healthy leg downward. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and it is made clear to you the health of its restoration: then apply a plaster and cotton, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And you must be cautious or fearful of the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too strong over them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days or three. If the bandage softens, bind it tightly: then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. If however he attempts to walk before that period, there is no security that the dislocation will not dissolve over him, and be corrupted, and not receive healing after that.
If a warm abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary to administer treatment for its reduction as previously described by us in another place regarding care and embrocation, until it is removed.
Regarding the dislocation of the toes and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them be aligned with slight extension without difficulty: and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot's arch: then it is necessary for the patient to sit with his foot on an even place on the ground, or on a board: and he should stand as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot forcefully until it returns and you see it now even, and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place under the arch of his foot a board that captures the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind it tightly for three days. Then loosen it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured from displacement.
Regarding the types of dislocations with wounds or fractures.
When something from that occurs and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows: and because of this, one should not presume to treat such a condition unless he is wise in the art through long experience, merciful, pious, cautious, not bold, nor presumptuous: and that he uses at the outset medicines which soothe abscesses, only warm ones: and leaves the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from peril with the mildness of the illness, and in which there appears some hope. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a warm abscess occurs.
If the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that soothes the abscess: and treat the wound with what is suitable for it from drying ointments.
If the dislocation is with fracture, and cavities separate occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do in that which we said in simple ailments from what preceded in their locations.
And be careful according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear: according to which my previous teaching was to you.
For it is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body among them in your time.
